MANILA, ilarch 16.â 0n Monday
afternoon I went aboard the
United States gunboat Laguna
de Bai and accompanied her on
a chase of the rebels. She first
steamed past the town of Pasig and
turned her Gatling guns on the insur
gents intrenched along the shore. The
insurgents were so- frightened that
women, children, horses, soldiers and
carabos fled in hundreds across the
open fields toward Laguna de Bai.
The gunboat ceased firing, but fol
lowed close behind the panic-stricken
crowd, driving them inland.
On Tuesday the gunboat Oesta joined
the Laguna de Bai. both under com
mand of Captain Grant. They made for
the lake channel, which had been
blocked by the insurgents, and finally
cleared a way. On the lake we chased
two sailing vessels; which were full of
insurgent troops- These vessels refused
to surrender and ran ashore, the troops
escaping in spite of our fire.
During Wednesday, while approach
ins: Santa Cruz, the richest city on the
lake, we raised a white flag when 500
yards from the shore. A large force of
insurgents is intrenched in front of the
town, but they refused, to confer with
us. Then we shelled their position ef
fectively and withdrew to the end of
the lake to await the landing of our
forces.
Major Rogers with a battalion of the
Twentieth Infantry, to-day attacked
Cainta. where the insurgents were in
trenched 1000/ strong- After a severe
fight the p:aÂ£e was taken and the in- ;
as org an ta driven back on the town of
Taytay. I
The troops first encountered tha rebel j
outposts in the dense jungies on the
banks of the river. The enemy was !
dislodged after half an hour's fighting. |
The Americans advanced in a splendid
manner under a heavy fire until ready
to volley the rebels from the trenches.
The latter had a great advantage and
dropped a number of our men. The
Americans charged across the rice
fields, making four advances on the
enemy, five hundred of whom were in
trenched, and in the face of a cross
fire.
Our troops carried the town after
four hours' fighting and burned the
outskirts, the rebels firing from the
windows and keeping up a running fire
in the streets. The Americans then

WILL SET A TRAP
TO CATCH AGUINALDO
WASHINGTON, March 16.â The cap
ture of Cainta, it is believed at the War
Department, indicates that it is Gen
eral Otis' plan to move up the Mara
quina or San Mateo River, thence to
Malolos. The opinion is now expressed
that it is General Otis' intention to
move the troops north along the San
Mateo at least as far as the town of
that name, which is some ten or twelve
miles from Cainta. By this movement
he will be able to approach the rebel
capital from three directions.
It is intimated that General H. G.
Otis will menace it from the south and
General Hale from the east, while Ad
miral Dewey will attack from the west.
Only the north will still be open for
the rebel retreat, and . that .->- be
closed before long by a force trans
ported by water to Dagupan at the
head of the Gulf of -gay on.
Unofficial advices from Manila say
that the Filipinos are simply aghast
at the tactics of the Americans. The
Spaniards, they claim, never attempted
such heroic measures as an attack by
night or in the hot midday. They al
ways took their night's rest and their
noon siesta, as gentlemen should. They
say that the Americans ought to have
sense enough to know better than to
expose themselves to the hot sun in the
middle of the day.
"The fact of the matter fa that Gen
eral Otis now controls the situation,'.'
said an officer with whom I talked.

and but a short strip of territory sepa

WASHINGTON, March IS.â A
cablegram received by General
Miles from Martinique announces
that ex-Secretary of State Sher
man Is better to-day.
WASHINGTON. March 15.â A tele
gram was received at the White House
to-day from Colgate Hoyt, who a a
traveling companion of ex-Secretary
Sherman. dated at Port de France,
island of Martinique, announcing that
Mr. Sherman is suffering from an at
tack of pneumonia and requesting that
he be brought to the United States on
a Government vessel. The message was
sent to the Navy Department and Sec
retary Long immediately gave the nec
essary order to comply with the wish.
It was found that the Chicago was j
just about due at Havana, and a cable
gram was sent to that point to have the i
ship proceed at once to Kingston, Ja
males to meet the Paris and take the :
patient off. Unless this is done Mr.
Sherman must remain on the vessel fur ;
two weeks or the excursion which the :
ship carries must be abandoned. The ;
Chicago is provided with medical offi
cers, so that the patient will have the
best of attention on his homeward
journey. He will probably be taken '
aboard the warship on Sunday or Mon
day and should arrive at Hampton ,
Roads in about a week. There he can
be taken aboard one of the Potomac
River steamships and brought to Wash- !
ington without much inconvenience.
Spinal Meningitis.
WOODLAND. March Id.â Several cases
of spinal meningitis are reported among
the children of this county. There is a
difference of opinion, but the prevailing
sentiment among physicians is that the
disease is contagious.

STRONG HAND
TO-BE FELT
BT CUBANS
The United States Will Per
mit No Triflijg by the
Assembly.
GOMEZ IS THE HEAD
Any Organized Body Trying to
Make Trouble Will Be Dis
persed as a Mob.
SrÂ«cial Dispatch to The Call.
NEW TORK. March 15.â Wash
ington correspondent of the Herald tel
egraphs: "Any communications made
by this Government through its officers
in Cuba will be made only to the inhab
itants of the island and not to any or
ganization pretending to represent
them. This is absolutely necessary,
and no other course can be pursued.
as there is no body politic in Cuba
coming from the people and represent
ing them by the popular will which can
be recognized."
This important statement was au
thorized at the War Department to
day. As a result of recent acts of the ;
so-called National Assembly the au- i
thorities have come to consider its \
members as mischief makers, instead
of representatives of the people of
Cuba. It is stated that General Brooke
will take no formal action relative to
the Assembly for the reason that such
action would be a recognition of that
organ:: It will simply be ordered
to disperse, as the police would disperse
a mob.
The authorities are paying no atten
tion to reports that the Assembly will
stir up the Cuban army to hostilities
against the United States. The influ
ence of Gomez is expected to prevent
â¢he troops from supporting the Assem
bly, and it is thought the Government
will be able to reach the Cuban people
through General Gomez, who will be
recognized as the representative of the
Cubans as individuals.
THE CUBAN LEADERS
MAY MAKE TROUBLE
HAVANA. March 16.â General Pedro i
Betancourt has arrived from Matanzas j
and conferred with General Pedro
Diaz, Jesus Monteagudo, Luis Sobau,
Alberto Nodarse and Francisco Pera- j
za. who last night decided to act in j
conjunction with him In condemning j
the action of the Military Assembly.
Considerable interest attaches to the
attitude they will adopt with respect to
General Gomez, because they command
over 20,000 Cuban soldiers. If they
adhere to Gomez it is asserted that
their action will probably be the As
sembly's deathblow. The general feel
ing is that they will support- him. but
they would like to secure the co-opera
tion of General Mayia Rodriguez, com
manding general in the provinces of
Pinar del Rio, Matanzas, Havana and
Santa Clara. This may prove difficult,
as he has already publicly expressed
his adherence to the Assembly.
Should Rodriguez continue to defend
the Assembly's course, and should these
generals declare in favor of Gomez,
trouble might follow. They are corps
commanders under him, but their I
united influence is considered stronger
than his. The meeting they had ar
ranged for this afternoon was post- j
poned.
The talk of General Gomez for Presi
dent of the Cuban Republic is increas
ing as a very natural reaction against |
the censures passed upon him by the j
Military Assembly.

MINERS MET
IN DEADLY
COLLISION
Americans and Canadians
Reported to Have Battled
on the Porcupine. *
Special Mapa-Xli to I* Call.
- . ..-â â â  â  i_S namw
NEW YORK. March 17.-A Van
couver. B. C, special to the World
says: Far in the* frozen north, a few
miles off the desolate Dalton trail, Ca
nadian and American miners have met
in deadly conflict. Four are reported
to have been killed outright and a
number of others are said to have been
seriously wounded. ,
That the bitter feeling between the
two nationalities has culminated in
actual bloodshed is regretted on all
sides. The disputed boundary line be
tween Alaska and Canada is the prin
cipal cause of it, although minor dis
putes assisted in bringing it about.
When the alien mining law of British
Columbia was enforced a few months
ago, the American miners left Atlin.
the new Canadian gold district, and
struck north. They found a short dis
tance off the Dalton trail on the Por
cupine River a district rich in placer,
gold. It was generally conceded that
the new placers were in American ter
ritory, and the miners vowed before
taking possession **** any Canadian,
be he mounted policeman or private
citizen, should not stake a claim.
Some of .the Canadian mounted po
licemen, taawever, did stake claims in
American territory and justified their
act by moving the Canadian flag from
.â  . -2
SOLDIERS TELLING
OF THE BAD BEEF
FED TO THEM
More Testimony Given by Offi
cers in Support of the Con
tention of Miles.
OMAHA, March 16.â The board of in
quiry appointed by the President to
look into the charges of General Miles
that the beef issued during the late
war was of an inferior quality, un
healthy and at times "embalmed" ar
rived this morning from Chicago, spent
the day in the city and left for Kansas
City this evening. The morning was
spent in South Omaha, where the plants
of Cudahy and Swift were inspected
and testimony taken, after which the
Investigation was continued In the
court-martial rooms at the headquar
ters of the Department of Missouri.
At both Swift's and Cudahy's the vis
itors were given every opportunity to
make a thorough inspection of the
plants. After lunch the board recon
vened at the army headquarters and
heard the testimony of soldiers Who
had been through the campaign at San
tiago. _ ,
Captain S. W. Dunning of the Six
teenth Infantry Bald that after their
arrival at Santiago the men complained
that they could not eat the meat and
did not "like it. They said the meat
spoiled as soon as opened. He was no.
rrÂ«pared to say whether any Blckness
occurred by reason of the use of the
canned beef. Complaints of refriger
ator beef were that it was spoiled and
the quartermasters were loth to take it.
His command was insufficiently ra
tioned from July 3 until it left Cuba.
The rations consisted of beef, hard
bread coffee and sugar, and when the
beef was not used it left them short.
Insufficient transportation caused part
of this shortage. On the voyage back
from Cuba canned beef was not eaten
Mount Pleasant, on the trail, so as to
make the line take in the rich part of
the district. They were speedily fol
lowed by at least forty Canadian
miners who all located good claims.
A fortnight ago about 100 American
miners held a meeting and decided to
send notices to all Canadians to leave
the country- The meeting was secret,
as aliens, according to American law.
are permitted to mine in Uncle Sam's
,_
territory.
Notices were duly sent out, but no
heed was paid to them. So the affair
rested, as far as the outside world was
concerned, until last evening when the
steamer Tees arrived from Lynn Canal
with a passenger on board direct from
the disaffected district. He is a Ger
man named Carl Slummerfelt, and he
brought news that a battle had actu
ally been fought between the American
and Canadian miners, resulting in a
number of casualties.
Slummerfelt's story is unconfirmed
except by the very serious reports re
ceived here about two weeks ago from
the district, which came from reliable
sources and predicted a clash if the
Canadians did not leave the territory:
"I left Porcupine River District about
two weeks ago," said Slummerfelt,
"and the day before I started there was
a bloody battle between the American
and Canadian miners. Notice was sent
out by the former that all Canadians
must go within five days. Supported
by a number of Canadian mounted po
lice, who had claims in the district, the
Canucks did not stir. Early cm the
morning of the sixth day the American
miners met and proceeded to the Cana
dian camp.
"I don't believe they intended blood
shed, although they were fully armed.
Before they could even state the object
of their visit, some one, I don't know
from which party, fired a shot, and
then every one seemed to be shooting.
Several rounds were fired and four men
I was told were killed outrightâ an
American and three Canadians.
"The battle was very brief and re
. suited in the Canadians, about fifty In
number, fleeing across the border. The
Americans then retired to their own
camp. I was informed that the fol
lowing Americans were leaders in the
battle, but cannot say for certain: C.
G. Lewis and Charles Leitch of Los
Angeles, W. S. Hawes of Minnesota, A.
McConaghy of Chicago and F. Will
borough of Denver.
"I did not ascertain the names of the
killed, as I passed hastily through the
American camp on my way out to the
coast. From my location, about fifteen
miles north of Porcupine River, I heard
that a squad of Canadian Northwest
mounted police had left Lake Tagish
for Porcupine, news of the fight having
reached them. The Americans are de
termined and I fear there will be an
other conflict. There is no doubt in
my mind that the new district is in
American territory, although I am not
an American."
Slummerfelt lived formerly a few
miles from Los Angeles, Cal. While
his story is not. confirmed, he is em
phatic in all his statements. He says
he is not anxious for notoriety, and
only told the story to reporters under
pressure. He is now with friends in
this city, but he will leave for the south
to-morrow.
PACIFIC UNDERWRITERS.
Important Matters Being Considered
by the Board.
DEL MONTE, March 16.â Board of
Fire Underwriters of the Pacific assem
bled here in executive session to dispose
of a large quantity of business which has
accumulated since the last convention
spent a busy day. Preliminary work. was
nearly all disposed of and the beginning
of definite action for results was reached,
although little along that line was accom
plished. The question of rates for San
Francisco, which was begun yesterday,
was again taken up at the morning ses
sion' to-day and was given more careful
consideration than at yesterday's delib
eration, but no definite action Was taken.
The matter will most probably reach a
vote to-morrow.
Three other topics of vital importance
were opened to-day. They were the ques
tion of competition of companies belong
ing to the board with non-board insurance
companies, rules for business interviews
with such non-board companies and the
question of representation of officers in
the board, that is, whether a man who
represents more than one company, as
many do, shall have more than one vote.
Much of the business of the day was done
through committees the work of, the
board as a whole being rather discussing
and suggesting lines - upon which future
action shall be taken. It is believed a
vote will be taken to-morrow on all four
matters now under consideration. ff-~'f

FOOR NEGROES
SHOT TO DEATH
A Fifth Will Die of His
Wounds.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PALMETTO, Ga., March 16.âMilitia
men and armed citizens are patrolling
the streets to-night to prevent an out
break of negroes, who are threatening
to destroy the town in revenge for the
lynching of several of their race, which
occurred shortly before daylight.
Masked men entered the village on
horseback early this morning and si
lently proceeded to a warehouse which
was used as a Jail. In it were confined
nine colored men charged, with incen
diarism.
The guards were overpowered and
four negroes were killed, one fatally
wounded and three slightly hurt. The
ninth escaped without a scratch.
The dead:
BUD COTTON.
HENRY BINGHAM,
TIP HOTSON.
ED BROWN.
Dying:
John Bigby.
Wounded:
Clem Watts, shot in the abdomen.
George Taylor, wounded in the thigh.
Isham Brown, shot in the body.
John Jamison, arm fractured.
Citizens are patrolling the main street
of the town and dispersing an occa
sional group of muttering negroes, who
seem determined to get some form of
revenge for the slaughter.
The .scene at Johnson's warehouse,
where nine negroes were confined, and
which is now filled with the dead and
dying, is a revolting and miserable one.
Blood covers the floor and blackened
walls are indented with rifle shots.
Wives and children or . negroes are
kneeling by their side in the dimly
lighted room and piercing the air with
cries and moans of anguish. â¢
The mob was composed of 150 men.
Where they came from is a mystery
so far as the people of the town know.
That some of the people of Palmetto,
but not the better class, were in the
mob goes without saying.
Every face was masked, and when
the warehouse was reached the special
guard of five men were covered with
out a word. In a minute the mob was
in the big warehouse and the fusillade
opened. The frightened negroes sent
up yell after yell, begging for mercy,
but it had no effect on the mob. When
its work was finished. the masked mob
turned and quickly disappeared as it
had appeared, on horseback. Two fires
of incendiary origin have occurred here
this year, one on February 23 and the
other flve days later. Fourteen busi
ness houses ln all were destroyed.
Nine negroes were arrested on suspi
cion and taken to Johnson's warehouse
to await preliminary trial, which was
to have taken place this morning. Yes
terday the leader of the gang, Bud Cot
ton, is said to have made a written
confession of the guilt of himself and
associates. He was the first to die in
the mob's bloody work. The negroes
frequently have .threatened in the last
week to burn Falrburn, the county seat,
and it is reported that the mob came
largely from that town.

ARMY BILL IN
THE REICHSTAG
Peace Footing Cut Down
Materially.;
REDUCED SEVEN THOUSAND
AFTER AMENDMENT THE MEAS
URE IS PASSED.
However, the German Government Is
Not Satisfied and Will Soon Re
new the Fight to Increase
the Troops.
Special Dispatch. to The Call.
BERLIN, March 16. â The
Washington telegram saying
that Mr. White will be ap
pointed United States Commis
sioner to the coming peace con
ference has caused a feeling
nearly approaching consterna
tion in the embassy. The Em
bassador knew nothing about it,
and seeing a number of matters
coming up just now between
Germany and the United States
the report was not credited.
BERLIN, March 16.â 1n anticipation
of the third reading of the army bill to
day both the Reichstag and the public
galleries of that house were packed.
The. various items of the estimates
were first discussed, and the proceed
ings were so turbulent that the presi
dent, Count yon Ballenstein, was re
peatedly compelled to call the house to
order.
When the army bill was taken up
Dr. Leiber, the Centrist leader, moved
the acceptance of the committee pro
posal to reduce the effective peace force
by 7000 men, adding a resolution to the
effect that the house in its willingness
in the event of the figure granted by
the committee proving inadequate, to
enter into fresh negotiations with the
Government.
The Minister of War, General yon
Gossler. urged the adoption of the en
tire Government demands and con
cluded with declaring that if the
whole peace effective force was not
voted now it must inevitably again
come before the house.
Prince Hohenlohe, the imperial Chan
cellor, said that In view of the mili
tary importance possessed by the in
formations approved by the committee
and inasmuch as their being carried
out could not be delayed, the Federal
Government accepted Dr. Lieber's mo
tion with the reservation that in con
formity with the readiness expressed
by the Reichstag to enter into fresh
negotiations the Government will intro
duce fresh proposals before the expira
tion of the agreed term, and from the
willingness with which the Reichstag
indorsed Lieber's resolution the Gov
ernment confidently deducted the be
lief that the imperial Government will
find acceptance in good time for the
accomplishment of the proposed organi
zation.
Hen-en yon Leftezow, Yon Kardoff,
Bassermann and Rikert accepted Dr.
Lieber's resolution in behalf of their
respective parties. Herr Richter de
clared that the acceptance of the Gov
ernment demand in its entirety would
be practically sanctioning military ab
solutism. yyyfyl
After further debate the general dis
cussion was closed and the articles of
the army bill were debated. Article 1,
which the committee did not alter, was
adopted. Article 2, as drafted by the
committee, was amended by Dr. Lie
ber's motion and adopted. Then the
whole bill was adopted by a vote of
222 to 132.
GIVEN THE POWER
TO SEARCH ALL HOUSES
If the Poundkeeper Tries to Enforce
Sausalitd's New Dog Ordinance
There Will Be Bloodshed.
SAUSALITO, March 16.â The despotic
powers conferred upon the town pound
keeper by an ordinance passed by th,
Town Trustees Monday night have roused
the residents to fever heat and threats
are heard that the first attempt to en
force its provisions will be followed by
bloodshed. â¢ â ; ,
The ordinance provides ostensibly for
the licensing of dogs, but does not stop
there. Unlicensed dogs are made unlaw
ful property, and to keep one is an of
fense for which imprisonment and fine
Is the punishment. Not content with this
gross violation of the law of property the
ordinance confers upon the poundkeeper
authority to search any house for the
purpose of ascertaining if the ordinance
fa being evaded. No preliminary proceed
ing is required. The poundkeeper can
walk into any person's house and insti
tute a search without asking leave or li
cense. , â¢
If any poundkeeper has the temerity to
exercise this unusual prerogative serious
trouble will follow. Captain Blddle, the
well-known pilot, In speaking of the mat
ter, said to-day: "Every man's house is
his castle, and if any attempt is made to
violate the privacy of my domicile I'll
resist with force. I have a dog whose
value is nominal, but so long as I keep
him within the enclosure of my property
the town authorities have no right to
meddle."
CAUSE OF THE RISE
IN SUGAR STOCKS
CHICAGO, March 16.â A special to the
Tribune from New York says: It leaked
out from Inner circles to-day as a feature
of the skyrocket work in the sugar stocks
that a meeting will take place here soon
at which all the glucose, sugar and coffee
interests of the United States are expect
ed to be present. It cannot be definitely
ascertained whether the principal owners
of these properties have Instigated the
conference or whether outside financial
interests are at the bottom of it.
It is known that the great fight between
the sugar trust and the Arbuckles has
reached a plane where the sugar trust Is
making Inroads on the package coffee
business of the Arbuckles. The Arbuckles.
on the other hand, with their great sugar
plant, are leaving no stone unturned to
break into the sugar trade of the trust.
An attempt, it is said, will be made to
regulate the business by leaving sugar to
the trust and coffee to the Arbuckles.
H. O. Havemeyer, president of the
American Sugar Refining Company, is
fatd to have acquired a large interest in
the glucose trust, and rumors of a com
bination of these interests are believed
to have caused to-day's rise in the stock
of both.

ALGER GETTING
READY FOR HIS
CUBAN JUNKET
Now the Secretary of War Is
Trying to Hide His Plans
From the Public.
NEW YORK, March 16.â The Herald's Washington correspondent
sends the following: Secretary Alger's junket to Cuba has not been
abandoned, notwithstanding the official statement he made a few weeks
ago as the result of the Herald's expose that on account of the pressure
of public business he would be compelled to remain in Washington.
I understand the Secretary has made arrangements to go to Cuba
about the middle of next week. He will not be accompanied by any
members o^he Senate and House Military Committees, but will be as
sisted in his "Investigation" of the conditions in Cuba and in his "in
spection" of troops by Victor J. Mason, his private secretary, and sev
eral other gentlemen. This time the Secretary may decide to pay for his
junket out of his own pocket, though as he at first determined It was in
tended to use the transport Berlin, now the Meade, and spend $200,000 of
Government money in making an "inspection" of Cuba and Porto Rico,
principally Havana and the battlefields of each island.
I was assured to-night that the Secretary will not leave on the
Meade, which will return from Havana by next week. His plans are
difficult to learn for the reason that he has given strict orders that
nothing be said concerning them to the press. He apparently fears
that their publicity will renew the outburst of public indignation caused
by the announcement of his intention last month, and compel him to
again postpone or perhaps abandon his trip. There seems to be a dis
position to believe that the Secretary will go to Tampa and there take
the Government transport, though he may have compunction enough to
take passage on board the regular steamer plying between Tampa and
Key West and Key West and Havana.
The Secretary is now on his way to Montreal for the purpose of dis
posing of private business affairs, and will return to Washington on
Sunday, when he will make final arrangements for his junket.
WALCOTT KNOCKS
OUT EDWARDS
Done in the Thirteenth
Round.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, March 16.â Walcott,
the colored pugilist, knocked out Billy
Edwards of Australia at the Broadway
Athletic Club to-night in the thirteenth
round of what was to have been a
twenty-round bout.
Walcott had Tom O'Rourke, George
Dixon, Jack Dougherty and Charley
Miller behind him as seconds. Edwards'
seconds were Dan Creedon, Tom Wil
liams and Charley J^eet. The principals
appeared to be in excellent shape. Wal
cott . was a 2-to-l favorite in the bet
ting. The opening round was not very
lively; in fact it was rather tame, and
honors were even, most of the punish
ment being delivered on the body.
.In the next Walcott had much the
better of the argument, landing rights
and lefts on the face, and body very
effectively,' while Edwards seemed un
able to -land a good ; fclow. : .
â¢'â  In the third round. Edwards was still
very, unsteady in his work and Walcott
was all over him continually. Edwards
was nearly sent through the ropes with
a terrible left on the wind and only
saved himself from getting out of
bounds by grabbing Walcott by the
legs early in the round. Edwards was
much distressed when he returned to
his corner. The Australian did a great
deal of sprinting in the fourth, but Joe
got to him frequently with both hands,
bearing in all the time. Edwards landed
a right on the body and a left on the
neck at close quarters, but Walcott
went back at him savagely. In a break
Walcott struck Edwards and there were
cries of "Foul," but the referee only
cautioned the negro and the men con
tinued, Walcott having everything his
own way.
The fifth round showed no harm in
Edwards and Walcott sailed into- him
with left and right swings which made
the Australian look like a novice. The
next round was only a repetition of the
previous ones, Walcott sending left jabs
to the wind and swinging his right to
the head and kidneys. Edwards grew
weak as the round progressed, but he
made a spurt at the close, sending left
and right to the head with little dam-
age.
Edwards took desperate chances in
the seventh. He fought very wildly,
while Walcott used his hands with good
judgment. Edwards hooked a hard left
on Joe's chin, jarring the negro's head.
Joe jumped back with a -vicious left
hano swing, which Edwards dodged.
Walcott, still on the aggressive,
forced his opponent all over the ring,
landing lefts and rights on the body
and head in the eighth. Once toward
the end of this round Edwards landed
a hard left swing on the side of Joe's
head which almost took him off his
feet. Joe righted himself quickly and
jumped back at Edwards, boring in
with both hands till the bell rang.
The ninth round found Walcott still
forcing matters. Billy made several
attempts to counter his opponent's
leads, but Walcott was too clever, and
smashed Edwards repeatedly on the
face with vicious lefts and jabs, forcing
him to the ropes frequently.
Edwards was certainly very game
and came up readily for the tenth
round. But his garrreness was of little
use against the sledge-hammer blows
which Walcott rained upon him. Ed
wards fell down in the middle of the
ring from a body blow and remained
down nine seconds. Walcott flew at
him like a gamecock when he got up
again and pummeled him hard and
fast till the gong sent them to their
corners.
Walcott opened the eleventh round
with a hard left on the face and Billy
clinched. Edwards swung his left to
the jaw and Walcott sent his loft to the
stomach and his right over the kidneys.
There was plenty of infighting, of
which Walcott always had the better.
Beginning the twelfth round the men
rushed to a clinch, after which Ed
wards sent in a hard left on the wind.
Walcott returned with a right in the
ribs and a left smash on the face, forc
ing Edwards to the ropes, where the
referee had to pull them apart. Ed
wards met Walcott suddenly with a
right swing on the jaw, which sent Joe
staggering fully ten feet to the ropes.
Edwards was too tired to follow up,
and Walcott came back like a cyclone,
mixing it up until the gong sounded.
Edwards landed a left swing on Wal
cott's head In the thirteenth, but Joe
sent back a left on the face, which put
Edwards on his back. Nine seconds
elapsed before Edwards got up. Wal
cott got him at close quarters and sent
him down again with a left on the body
and fell on top of him. As soon as they
shaped again Walcott rushed and
Jabbed his left to the face, following it
up with a right on the point of the jaw,
which sent Edwards down and out,
with his head hanging over the bottom
rope. Edwards had to be carried !to
his corner, where he soon revived.
Walcott was declared the winner. Time
of round, 2:48. .V-
INDIAN SCHOOL
AT YUMA BURNED
Superstitious J^edskjns fire Sus
pected of Having Started
*
the Fire.
5
LOS ANGELES, March 16.â A fire which was discovered in the cloth- ',
ing room of the Fort Yuma Indian school, situated across the Colo- ;
rado River from Yuma, Ariz., destroyed two buildings containing the <
girls' dormitory, dining-room, kitchen and exhibition room. The school J
is laboring under many disadvantages, owing to two factions among the ]
Yuma Indians, one opposed to sending children to school and the other !
favoring. An epidemic of measles has caused the death of thirty-five \
pappooses in the past month. Six deaths occurred in the school. As the ;
Indians usually destroy houses in which persons die, the cause of the fire j
is attributed to the superstition of the Indians, who hoped to free the ;
tribe of the evil spirit, which they say brings disease and causes some of ;
the tribe to follow the white man's methods. ]
DEATH CLAIMS
"OLD HUTCH"
Close of the Career of
B. P. Hutchinson.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CHICAGO. March Benjamin P.
Hutchinson, the former Board of Trade
operator and packer, died of heart fail
ure at 5:30 o'clock this evening at the
Lakeside Sanitarium, Lake Geneva.
Wis. He was 70 years old. The re
mains will be brought to Chicago for
interment.
Mr. Hutchinson's health began to fail
rapidly three years ago, and during the
last two years he has been at the sani
tarium where he died.
Mr. Hutchinson was at the height of
his fame in 1888, when he ran his corner
in September wheat. After that began
his decline, and the financial end came
in 1891. His last deal was a big one,
he being on the wrong side of the mar
ket for $2,000,000. Financially crushed,
he left town and went to New York,
where he opened a second-hand store.
He gave up that and returned to Chi
cago, where he traded, ln a small way
when he could get people interested in
his schemes, but his career may be said
to have closed with the big deal of '91.
No one knew* how much he was
worth. He would never talk about pri
vate affairs. The estimates of his
wealth when at his nelght place the
amount anywhere from $2,000,000 to
$16,000,000. A conservative estimate
places his richest days at $4,000,000 to
$6,000,000. ;
WILL BE MARRIED IN
THE CONSERVATORY
Latest Edict Concerning the Marriage
of Young Vanderbilt and
Miss Fair.
NEW YORK, March 16.â William K.
Vanderbllt and Miss Virginia Fair are
to be married in the conservatory. This is
the latest edict. Father Murphy will per
form the ceremony. The conservatory
was added by Mr. and Mrs. Herman Oel
rlchs to their dwelling at the northeast
corner of Fifth avenue and Fifty-seventh
street during the summer of 1897. The
room will be arranged as much as pos
sible like a Gothic chapel with a central
aisle. The conservatory at the end will
be the chancel. In this will be placed an
altar with Its tall lighted wax candles,
in the midst of a tiny forest of Easter
blooms.
The wedding will be at noon. The
bride will be attended only by her maid
of honor, Miss Mary Baldwin Tolfree.
Mr. Carroll of Harvard University, a
classmate of the bridegroom, will be the
best man.
After the ceremony the bridal break
fast will be served at small tables in the
dining-room, hall and reception-room on
the ground floor. Later Mr. and Mrs.
Vanderbilt will go to Newport in a pri
vate car. .-â¢:

SCURVY RAGING
ON COPPER RIVER
Deaths From the Disease
and Exposure.
PERISH ON VALDES GLACIER
SAD FATE OF SIX MEN WHO
WERE AFFLICTED.
Two California Gold Hunters Among
the Victims of the Disease, and
There Are Many Other
Sufferers.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SEATTLE, March 16.â News that
scurvy was raging among the pros
pectors of Copper River, Alaska, and
that six men perished from cold on the
Valdes glacier in the flrst days of
March was brought here to-night on
the steamer Excelsior from the mouth
of Copper River. She brought nearly
one hundred passengers, 40 per cent
of whom were suffering from scurvy.
Following is a list of those who per
ished on the glacier:
ADOLPH EHRHARDT, New York.
MAXIMILIAN MILLER, New York.
ALFRED ALLEMAN, New York.
DR. EDWIN LOGAN, Denver.
RUDOLPH ELLERKAMP, Louis
ville, Ky.
AUGUSTUS SCHULTZ, New York.
All the bodies except that of Dr. Lo
gan were recovered and buried at Val
des. Ehrhardt, Miller and Alleman
were members of the Scientific Pros
pecting Company of New York. â '-<-.â¢â¢'
They were all in one party. Late in
February when they were encamped
on the other side of the glacier, Ehr
hardt and Miller were taken sick with
the scurvy. It was decided to bring
them out to Valdes. They were strap
ped down on sleds and were drawn by
the others. The party successfully,
crossed the summit and had started to
cross the glacier when a blizzard came
up. On the second of March Dr. Bar
rett, who has a camp between the
glacier and the summit, came into
Valdes and inquired whether the party
had arrived there. They had passed
his place and he was fearful that they
had been lost in the storm. A party
of ten left Valdes to search for the
men. It was a bitterly cold day. On
the fourth bench of the glacier they
found Ehrhardt and Miller strapped to
their sleds. Their bodies were not stiff,
indicating that they had been dead but
a short time. Near by Schultz was
found buried in a hole. His right hand
was stretched above his head. At the
foot of the bench the bodies of Eller
kamp and Alleman were found in a
crevice. The body of Dr. Logan was
not found, and it is supposed that he
wandered away from the trail in search
of help.
When the steamship sailed ten deaths
had taken place, dating back three
months, and over fifty cases, many of
which were unusually severe, probably
fatal, were known to exist. According
to reports the epidemic extends over
the entire section .of what may be
spoken of as the Copper River Valley,
and it seems to be increasing.
The list of the dead, complete as can
be obtained from those returning, is as
follows:
S. MILLIGAN, Los Angeles. â¢
JOHN ROHR, Honolulu.
NICK URBAN, Pittsburg.
JACK HADEN, San Jose, Cal.
D. C. CUSHMAN, Meadville, Mo.
DAN MANARD, Meadville, Ma.
It is said that there has been one
death at Twelve-mile City and three at
least at Copper City. Returning pros
pectors are unable to give the names
of these victims.
Among those who are sick with
scurvy are the following: W. O. H.
Cully, Warrenburg, Mo. ; Dan Cash
man, Chicago; Dr. Winslow. Los An
geles; Joe Markowitz, San Francisco:
Valentine Schick, Santa Fe, N. M. ;
George Corley, Hartford, Conn. : San-
Brady, Elliott, Iowa; George Lowe. El
liott, Iowa; Julian Mueller. Miami.
Fla.; Russell Briggs, San Francisco:
W. S. Berry, Wheatland, Cal., and W.
B. Crane, Lee Summit! Mo.
Another fatality reported by the Ex
celsior's passengers is the freezing to
death of Peter Benson of Madison. Wis.
Benson had started from the interior
to Valdes over what is known as the
summer trail. In crossing a small
creek the ice broke and he was wet
from head to foot. Before he could get
to a fire he was frozen stiff.
Scurvy is reported to be present in
all the camps in the interior, and many
miners are coming to the coast either
to escape it or to recover from its ef
fects. Six deaths have occurred during
the winter. At Copper Center there
are twenty scurvy patients in the hos
pital. The last report from Dr. Town
send was to the effect that they were
all improving and that he expected no
further fatalities.

DELAY WORRIES
THE DEPOSITOR.
Tangled State of Union
Bank's Affairs.
BRASLAN'S WRIT GRANTED
DIRECTORS RESTRAINED FROM
TAKING ANY ACTION.
People Who Put Their Money Into
the Defunct Concern Have Some
Cause for Becoming
Uneasy.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE, March 16.â Justice Beat
tie of the State Supreme Court to-day
granted the writ of certiorari asked for
by Charles P. Braslan to review the ac
tion of Judge Lorigan in appointing a
board of directors to settle up the busi
ness of the defunct Union Savings
Bank. The directors are also restrained
from acting until after the matter has
been decided. Braslan was elected a
director immediately after he failure
and refused to resign when the others
did. Judge Lorigan ignored his stand
ing as a director and appointed an en
tire new board.
The 1700 and more depositors of the
Union Savings Bank are becoming un
easy at the continual delays occurring.
And again they realize that the assets
of the bank will never pay them dollar
for dollar. Assessments will have to
be levied and suits brought to collect
them. This will take time. During the
last few days an unusually large num
ber of depositors have called upon At
torney J. R. Welch, the counsel of the
organized depositors, and requested
that some action be taken at once to
rush matters. Some favor both civil
and criminal actions against the old
board of directors.
â¢ Attorney Welch stated this afternoon
that the granting of Braslan's writ
meant considerable delay. He said the
easiest way out of the matter would be
for one of the directors appointed by
Judge Lorigan to resign and then the
rest might elect Braslan to the va
cancy. If the Supreme Court sustains
Braslan the whole board will be illegal
and the whole matter will have to be
gone through again.
If Directors Rea, Simpson and Dun
lop owe the bank money, and Welch
said he was satisfied they did, then
they should never have been appointed
directors. Simpson and Rea hold a few
shares of stock in trust, while Dun
lop's stock has been acquired since he
was named as a director.
The board of directors of the bank
held a meeting this morning, the full
directorate being present. A letter was
received from the Bank Commissioners
acknowledging notice of their organi
zation and stating that they would be
authorized in a few days to go ahead
with the bank's affairs. Adjournment
was taken till the call of the chair,
which, through the action of Judge
Beattie, has been indefinitely post
poned.
Developments of the past few days
! show that the assets of E. B. Howard,
i the fruit shipper accused of wrecking
! the Union Savings Bank, amount to
practically nothing. These have been
j placed at about $125,000 in his schedule
of bankruptcy, while the bank has al
! ways claimed they would be sufficient
j to pay off the securities if properly
t handled. Now it is asserted by those
j claiming to know that the assets will
I amount to about $12,000, or just about
| enough to pay the costs of bankruptcy.
j The bank claims Howard's indebted-
J ness amounts to $286,000, with nearly
$20,000 interest. Howard claims $225,000
of this amount had been wiped out.
Most of Howard's assets consisted of
dried fruit in the hands of Eastern
i commission men, who had advanced
j some 75 per cent on the shipments.
| When Howard failed the commission
I houses disposed of the fruit right away
| for just enough to cover advances. This
1 leaves nothing to be expected from this
! source. Some $40,000 worth ; of dried
i fruit in the hands of the Sorosis Fruit
: Company, claimed by Howard, has been
| disputed by the company and this
amount of the assets will also be lost.

"It Must Be the Mexican or No Senator," Cries the Corpo
ration, and the People Prefer That the State
Go Unrepresented.
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRAMENTO. March 16.âDan
iel M. Burns will not be the next United States Senator from
California. After a nine weeks' campaign of bluff and bluster he
finds that lie is at last forced into a position where the lamentable
weakness of his following must become apparent. Even his
closest friends and most stanch supporters are forced at this, the
eleventh hour of the Senatorial contest, to admit that their mas
ter no longer has a chance to win. Of the other candidates, both
Barnes and Grant express confidence in their ability to secure
election. How much of their confidence is Inspired by conditions
favorable to either of them will be made plain by the events of the
next forty-eight hours.
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March 10.â Wild reports of
changes and combinations are in the
air to-night It is difficult to get the
truth from the multitude of conflicting
rumors, but the judgment of the clear
est-headed men at this time justifies
the prediction that no Senator will be
chosen at this session of the Legisla
ture.
The defeat of D. M. Burns cannot
longer be doubted by those who were
most enthusiastic and zealous in his
support. Twenty-seven members of
the Legislature may go down clinging
to the wreck of his political ambition.
Early in the session his supporters pro
claimed "Burns or no Senator." The
good of the Republican party never cut
a figure in their calculations. The de
mand of the people of California for the
election of a clean and able Senator
excited in their minds only a sneer of
derision. The railroad programme was
Dan Burns for Senator, and that was
all they knew or cared to know.
The judgment roll will be made up
by the people of California, The names
of able men were presented as candi
dates, but the programmers failed to
rind merit in their candidacy. The peo
ple will ask why a Senator was nut j
chcsen and will be truthfully told that
the railroad attempted to foist Burns
en the Legislature and that .he men of
â¢ character, ability and integrity resented
the interference of the corporation in
ths political affairs of the State, hold-
Ing that a vacancy for two years in
the Senate was far preferable to the
election of Burns for six years. The
men of California who have stood up so
manfully and nobly against the election
of the railroad candidate will be ap
plauded by the people tut the course
they have pursued.
â¢ The chief touts are in the depths of
despair to-night. All day long Burns
has been at room 40 of the Capitol
building sending out requests for per
sonal interviews with members of the
Senate and Assembly. It is said that
he is anxious to obtain directly from
"the legislators an expression of opin
ion on the subject of a caucus to break
the deadlock. His dejected followers
"who have illy abandoned hope still
assume a confident front and boast to
night, as Grant's men boasted last
night, of great gains on the next bal
lot.
There are whispers of something to
happen to-morrow. it is surmised that
Burns may suggest the withdrawal of
all the candidates and the introduc
tion of a new man. Legislators who
- nre in a position to form the best judg
ment of the situation assert that the
fight will be conducted to the finish on
the lines now established.

ing not to oppose the resolution to ad
journ sine die on Saturday night next.
and so there was practically no objec

sembly measure providing for the final

nonading the Barnes and Burns camps

LEGISLATURE WILL
NOT HELP THE
RACE GAMBLERS
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
! MENTO, March 16.â The Legislature
will have none of the proposition made
, by the promoters of the gambling game
at Ingleside to enact a j law that will
' have the effect of nullifying the action
taken Monday by the Board of Super
visors of San Francisco. The racing
combination was given to understand
this yesterday, but it was not until
Corrigan and his reinforcements ar
rived on the ground last night and were
made aware of the situation that they
came to the conclusion that their case
is hopeless so far as the Legislature is
concerned. No legislator could be found
who would stand sponsor for any meas- |
ure antagonistic to the interests of the j
people of the metropolis so far as they .
are affected by the pernicious racing
game at Ingleside.
When they came to Sacramento seek
ing relief the Ingleside gamblers ex
pected to receive support and comfort
from the representatives of the Cali
fornia Jockey Club, who are managing
the racing game at Emeryville. In this
they were disappointed, and because of j
it a merry war is now being waged ;
between the opposing factions, with '
the advantage on the side of the Em- i
eryville gamblers, who aspire to exclu
sive control of the game that is filling
the penitentiaries with criminals.
The reasons that have actuated the
California Jockey Club people to with- i
hold their support from the effort being ;
made by the Ingleside gamblers to set i
aside the will of the Board of Super
visors of San Francisco are made ap
parent when it 12 stated that the con
tract under which the two tracks are ;
now operating and by the provisions
of which racing shall be conducted at
each track on alternating dates during
the season, expires in May. With the
expiration of this, contract will-end any
obligation of a formal nature now ex
isting between the owners and man-
I which racing shall and Emeryville
h track or. alternating dates during
. exi-iivs in May. With the
(ration .ntract will end any :
igation of a formal nature now ex
ng between the owners and man
rs of the Ingleside and Emeryville j
tracks.
The Interdicting of bookmaking and
pool selling at Ingleside will have the !
effect of closing the track. Then when
the contract now existing expires the
Emeryville people will be able to race \
every day in the year and will corral
within their own treasury the ill-gotten
gains that are now divided between the ;
two tracks.
Under conditions as they now exist
both tracks are bonanzas, therefore it
is not to be expected that the Emery
ville gamblers will "play fair," and by
doing so surrender an opportunity now ;
afforded to "hog" the profits and double
their income. Because of these facts
the Emeryville gamblers have refused
to aid the Ingleside gamblers, and with
the falling out between them comes the
opportunity for honest men to come to !
their dues.
A GENERAL SCRAMBLE
AMONG SENATORS
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March 16.â The Senate is getting
demoralized. Having fixed the hour for
adjournment, the Senators are all work
ing to secure the passage of their indi
vidual measures, and It is now a poor
plan to look for a Senator in his own seat.
A large delegation of Assemblymen Is in
constant attendance, and legislation is
rushing.
The lobby Is filled with underworked
attaches, watching bills which will pro
vide for their payment, friends of Sena
tors who want bills jumped forward on
the files and brought at once to considera
tion; more friends of other Senators who,
having kept bills at the lower ends of the
files during the greater part of the ses
sion, now want to make sure they will
not rise from the several graves so neatly
provided for them. They have all been
here for weeks, but now they have sud
denly jumped into aggressive activity,
and every one Is constantly on the move
Headed by Charles L. Patton. the San
Francisco Exposition delegation distrib
uted itself among the Senators during the
afternoon and added to the subdued buzz
of conversation that played an accom
paniment to the orators who would talk
despite the flight of time. The Scott
forces three in numberâ gathered around
Davis' desk and talked earnestly. Bett
man between rollcalls polled the Senate
or tried toâ on Rlckard's boxing bill
that has lately wandered in after weath
ering the Assembly; a delegation of
teachers tarried long enough to raise ap
plause when the appropriation for the
San Francisco Normal School was passed*
the major and Jake, his man Friday'
spend ln the lobby all the time they can
spare from that of the Assembly, and al
together, like a "ringed" tree, the Senate
is blossoming furiously before it dies.
After considerable discussion the Senate

come tax bill was defeated. The hill was
not considered exactly suitable, and the
vote on it was to many of the Senators
as much an expression of opinion on the
subject of an Income tax as it was a vote
on the measure.

mission cut a rift ln the monotony of the

one, for his bill was snowed under. Ho
wanted to reconsider, but announced his
intention too late and was declared out of
order. On speaking to his bill Boyce took
occasion to roast the Code Commission

reply to his remarks and the bill was

own request excused from voting.
Boyce declared that at the last session
of the Legislature the report of the com-

MUST BE SIGNED
CALL HEADQUARTERS, .SACRA
MENTO, March 16.â Senator Morehouse's
little bill designed to compel the down
trodden reporter to sign his own stuff,
; and, with the proprietor of his paper, as
' sume responsibility for it, passed the As
sembly to-day on final passage by a vote
of 47 to 20.
It did not pass, however, without a good
stiff fight. Melick of Pasadena, Sanford
of Mendocino, Feliz of Monterey and a
half dozen others, among them Wade of
Napa, all lined up against the measure
and fought It until it was carried. :
Melick offered amendments striking out
some of the more radical features of the
measure, which were voted down. Sanford
made a play for technical delay, and
Feliz of Monterey ably seconded him.
Johnson of Sacramento made it the oc
casion for one of his famous anti-news
paper dissertations. He said the bill was
not designed to hurt the bright young
men who do the writing; on the contrary.
It would improve their position. It was,
however, designed to compel the "cow
ards and assassins" who run the San
Francisco dallies and persist in showing
i up all sorts of legislative crookedness to
i take the responsibility.

I apparent. : - : '_ '-Y-.-.-y - \ -~y fy .

I moving the previous question, but San
' ford forced him to withdraw and consent
1 to ten minutes more of argument, when
! the bill agidn came up at 2 o'clock in the
! afternoon. Then It was passed and or
j dered returned to the Senate.
ASSEMBLY BILLS ONLY
GET SENATE NOTICE
CALL HEADQUARTERS. SACRA
MENTO, March 16.â The Senate has com
mitted the last act which binds it to an
adjournment. It adopted a special Joint
rule this evening regarding the considera
tion of bills which closes the doors to the
consideration of any but Assembly bills.
The rule provides that no Assembly bills
shall be considered by the Assembly and
no Senate bills shall be considered by the
Senate, except bills from each House in
conference, after midnight to-night. Ex
ception is made, however, to the appro
priation bill, the tax levy bill, amend
ments to the county government, consti
tutional amendments and bills detailed by
notices of motions to reconsider. By
amendment in the Senate claim bills, for
repairs on State bunuings and for the re
lief of State commissions, were also ex
empted from, the terms of the rule. As
amended the rules were adopted. - .- >.
As a consequence all this evening the
Senate was busy rushing Senate bills
through on urgency resolutions and off
the urgency file. It was the last chance,
and business ran past the Senate as fast
as â¢ the secretary could draw the : bills
from the desk. The whole special file
was finished up, and that included f all
_. ......
the legislation of their own the Senators
cared particularly about.
BURNS' LATEST
SCHEME IS TO
TAKE A RECESS
The Mexican Will Try Illegal
Means to Prolong the
Joint Convention.
CALL HEADQUARTERS. SACRA
MENTO. March 16.â of the last j
schemes of the Mexican candidate for j
forcing the Legislature to elect him
United States Senator is the prolonga
tion of the joint convention on Satur- !
day. If the voting to-morrow does not j
result in bringing to his standard any
new votes he proposes to take several
ballots on the last day and force a
recess until evening, thus getting a
final opportunity to threaten and ca
jole the members.
There is considerable doubt, however,
whether under the Federal statute (re
quiring the Legislature to meet dally
and take at least one ballot for Sen- i
ator) a candidate chosen after such a j
recess would be legally elected. There '
is no instance where that procedure j
has been attempted. The purpose of
the law is to compel a meeting at noon i
on each day. The statute was enacted :
to prevent deadlocks resulting from
different political parties getting con
trol of the houses and refusing to meet
in joint convention. This often hap- I
pened where violent partisans pre- j
ferred no Senator to the election of one
belonging to the party which in joint
convention would have a majority.
No such thing as a recess is con
templated by the law and no limit is
by it placed upon the number of bal
lots which shall be taken. There Is no
doubt that the joint convention may
vote as many times as it pleases, and- â 
for that purpose it may sit all day. j
but whether it can take a recess and j
meet again is doubtful. Indeed, a ;
recess in parliamentary law Is prac- |
tically an adjournment. No provision j
is made in the law for a recess. |
A meeting must be held at noon each ;
day, and "if no person receives a ma
-1 jority on the first day the joint Assem
bly shall meet at 12 o'clock noon of each
succeeding day during the session of
the Legislature and shall take at least ;
one vote until a Senator is elected."
This law has been held to limit the
action of the Legislature, and in the '
absence of provision for a recess none
is legal. <*,
If the Burns touts attempt to bulldoze
members in this way their scheme will
be stoutly resisted by the followers of <
I Grant, Barnes and Scott, and if they
vote solidly it will not be possible to
carry it out. The suggestion, coming
as it does from Burns, shows merely '
how desperate the Mexican is getting
in the last hours; like a drowning man,
j he is now grabbing at straws, no mat
: ter how elusive. No intelligent person
can see what possible good it will do
him to set apart a few hours on the
i last day in which to exercise his final
! dying tactics.
COUNTY DIVISION
BILL KILLED
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, March Assemblyman Bur
nett's county division bill was killed in
the Assembly this afternoon and for an- !
other two years the warring factions of
San Luis Obispo County will have to con
sume their own smoke and keep their
knives in their sleeves.
Incidentally there ls now no law, as
there has not been for these two years
past, by which counties rent by inter
necine strife can get away from them
selves by dividing up their governments.
Mr. Burnett's bill was not lost without
a hard fight and some interesting debate
upon some of its peculiar provisions. One
of its most prominent sections is as fol
lows:
Section Iâ New counties in this State may,
from time to time, be formed and created from
portions of one or more counties already in
existence In the manner set forth in this act;
provided, however, that no new county shall
be established which shall reduce any county
to a population of less than SOOO or which
shall reduce the assessed value of any old
county more than one-half, as shown by the
last assessment thereof made previous to the
formation of such new county; nor shall any
new county be formed containing a less popu
lation than 5000. nor shall any line therecf
pass within five miles of the county seat of
any county proposed to be divided: and every
county which shall be enlarged or created from
territory taken from any other county or coun
ted shall be liable for a Just proportion of the
existing debt and liabilities of the county or
counties from which such territory shall be
taken.
Mr. Burnett spoke at some length to his
bill, making the basis for his argument
that the State is at present without law
or means for creating new counties when
they may become necessary. He did not
emphasize the fact that his measure was
drafted primarily for the benefit of his
own county, San Luis Obispo.
Know-land of Alameda made a strong
and excellent speech in opposition to the
measure. He stated that he believed the
bill had been introduced in the best of
faith by the gentleman from San Luis
Obispo. Mr. Burnett, part of the people
from that county desiring the measure.
I He believed, however, that an overwhelm
i ing majority of the counties throughout
| the State were opposed to county division

priation bill did not require ten minutes

A NEW MOVE STARTED
TO HELP GRANT'S CAUSE
LOS ANGELES, March 16.â Grant j
workers who are most particularly op- j
posed to Burns are endeavoring to con- i
vince Bulla voters to see the error of
their way and go to Grant. Cards bear
ing this brief but significant message,
"Having done your duty to Mr. Bulla,
you should now support U. S. Grant, a
Southern California man," will be sent
to Sacramento to break the deadlock. j
These blanks with .- envelopes for return {
to Los Angeles were sent to each Repub- j
lican who was a delegate in the county 'â 
convention that instructed for Bulla.
Of these ninety-seven, properly signed,
have reached the local headquarters of j
this latest Grant movement. Besides 1
these, there are on file in the same of
fice, awaiting transit to the north,
enough telegrams and letters of even
tenor to make a total of 325. Suggestions
for the transfer of fealty from the Bulla
to the Grant banner come from all quar
ters, and local managers declare they
could easily secure a majority of the 700
delegates and expect that the Southern
California delegation in Sacramento will
see by these various indications how the
wind blows and swing their support from
Bulla to Grant.
JOHNSON LOSES
A FAT LEGAL FEE
CALL HEADQUARTERS, . SACRA
MENTO, March 16.â Assemblyman John
son's attempt to lay hold of his legisla
tive bootstraps and lift himself toward a
fat attorney's fee was rudely stopped by
the Senate to-day by the denial of a sec
ond reading for his bill, No. 908.
Johnson's bill is to make certain per
sons now classed as incompetent wit
nesses in certain actions competent when
called by the executor -or administrator
of an estate in a suit against such estate.
There is not much to the bill until it
becomes known that Johnson is attorney
for the executors of two big estates now
being sued, and. curiously enough, if he
could only get the testimony of certain
witnesses now incompetent he would
stand a much better chance of winning,
and if his bill, No. 908, is read correctly
it will be found to allow such witnesses
as Johnson wants to give testimony. It
is good to be both lawyer and legislator.
The bill was denied second reading on
the recommendation of the committee
that it do not pass.

WOULD AMEND SANTA
FE PURCHASE BILL
Mayor Phelan. Francis Pope and others,
representing the anti-funding committee
organized some years since to fight the
Southern Pacific Company, will appear
before Governor Gage in Sacramento this
morning.
They go to plead with the chief execu
tive to refer back for reconsideration the
measure known as the Santa Fe purchase
bill. The members of the anti-funding
committee profess to believe that the
measure is intended to aid, at some future
date, ColiiÃ P. Huntington and the South
ern Pacific Company, of which he is
president. The proposed amendment to
the bill is intended to prevent the pur
chase of a leased line which is also a
competing line. This is especially de
signed to "prevent tho Southern Pacific
purchasing the Central Pacific.
Mayor Phelan had several important en
gagements In this city last night which
precluded the possibilty of his going to
Sacramento on the evening train. In order
'to fulfill the obligations he felt rested in
â¢him he attended the various functions at
which he was to preside and then boarded
a special train which would land him in
Sacramento in due time for this morning's
meeting. The changes in the bill sug
gested by the anti-funding committee and
drafted by Attorneys Murphy and Pope
are as follows:
Strike out all of the bill after the word
'â¢and'" ln line thirty-three and Insert ln
lieu thereof the following:
Provided, however, that such purchase or
lease shall in no wise affect the power of
this State over such sold or leased corporate
franchises a:. '. properties or the power of
this State to alter, revise, amend or repeal
the charter of the corporation the prop
erty of which is sold or leased; and tfcie
purchasing corporation .snail be subject to
the same limitations and obligations as to
the capita! stock and bonded indebtedness
as to all the llnÂ°s of railroad and other
property connected therewith within this
State and all other limitations and obliga
tions as if the franchises and properties
were still i assessed by the grantor or lessor,
and the grantee or lessee shall be considered
to have purchased the corporate franchise
of the grantor or lessor: provided, further,
that this -itate shall have the power to fix
and determine the rates for earning pas
sengers and freight over such purchased or
leased lines; and further provided that this
act shall net permit the purchase by any
railroad corporation of the franchises and
properties of any competing railroad.
HIS CRUELTY WAS
MORE THAN CRUEL
MRS. WILSON IS GRANTED A !
DECREE OF DIVORCE.
She Tells a Sad Story of Abuse by
Her Worthless and Inhuman
Husband.
Judge Daingerfield has granted Julia A.
Wilson a divorce from Charles W. Wil
son, shipping clerk of J. A. Folger & Co.,
on the ground of extreme cruelty. That
the sympathy of womankind will be ex
tended to Mrs. Wilson for her misfortune
in having been "her husband's wife" there
can be no doubt. When Mrs Wilson took
the stand on her own behalf she told a
story, which was corroborated in detail,
that shows Wilson to be the possessor
cf an Inhuman disposition almost beyond
imagination.
"I married my husband In September of ,
1896," said Mrs. Wilson, "and almost im- j
mediately he began to abuse me. In De
cember of 1897 I made known to him that
I was soon to become a mother.- He Im- '
mediately demanded that I see that he '
was not burdened with a child. I refused
to consider his cruel. inhuman demand, i
and then his anger overcame him. ' He i
said that he would torture me every min
ute of my life unless 1 did as he bade me. i
I refused, and then time after time he
would teli me that my child would be an
idiot, that it would come Into the world '
deformed and forever a sorrowful burden
upon me. His words became a torture to :
me. and the effect of the nervousness his :
words caused me I was afraid would re- !
suit in proving his statements only too i
true; but my fears were groundless.'
"Shortly after this he came home In- j
toxicated one evening arid immediately !
went In 'search of his razor, saying that
he was going to cut his throat and 'end it : .
all.' Knowing where the razor was, Ij
hurriedly got it ar.d hid it under the I
Stair carper. When he found his razor j
was gone ha went in search of a' knife,
and when I got in front of him to prevent
him carrying out his purpose he jammed
me against the doorsill and choked .me 1
severely. Finally he abandoned his
search for a knife, concluding that life ; .
was still worth the living. . -. â¢ â  :.â¢

S. P. CONNOR KILLED BY
A SIDEWALK ELEVATOR
A WINE CELLAR FOREMAN IS
CRUSHED TO DEATH.
Fatal Accident Occurs at 1049 Mar
ket Streetâ the Wrong
Chain by Mistake.
S P Connor, foreman of a wine cellar j
owned by the Mount Diablo Wine Com- â 
pany at 1049 Market street, was caught
in a sidewalk elevator at that place short
ly before 6 o'clock last evening and so
badly crushed .that he died before as
sistance could reach him.
No one was near the elevator when the
accident happened, but lt Is believed that
Connor pulled the wrong chain, raising
the elevator instead of lowering it as he
Intended. He had been working for the
wine company but two weeks, and it was
his habit in the evening to lower the iron
doors on the sidewalk from the floor of
S. P. CONNOR.
the elevator. Usually when performing
this duty he was assisted by one of the
other employes, but last night he at
tempted to do it without assistance.
Peter Glos had been helping Connor
about the cellar a short time before the
accident. Connor remarked to him that
it was about time to close up and went
toward the elevator. A few minutes af
terward Glos heard a noise that sounded
like the gasp of a dying man and, going
to the front of the cellar to investigate,
found Connor tightly wedged in between
the floor of the elevator and the Iron
doors. Glos and S. M.ilssing, another em
ploye, lowered the elevator and placed the
dying man on the floor. A physician was
s,ummoned, but Connor died before any
thing could be done for him.
The proprietors of the wine cellar knew
nothing of the dead man's family, as he
had been working for them but a short
time. Late last night a son appeared at
the Morgue and Identified the body.
Some of the employes of the wine com
pany stated to Deputy Coroner La Combe
that the elevator had been out of order
for some time, but others are of the opin
ion that the accident was due to the fact
that Connor pulled the wrong' chain.
Deceased resided at 1770 Howard street.
He was 56 years old and leaves a widow
and several grown children. He was a
wine expert and had recently returned
from Napa, where he had been connected
with a vineyard.
BALDWIN ANNEX LEASED.
' Cafe and Billiard Parlors Will Be
Opened During April.
James Orndorff and George Schmidt
have leased the Baldwin Annex and will
utilize the premises for a cafe and billiard
parlor. Since the destruction of the Bald
win Grotto an air of desolation has set
tled over that neighborhood, and these
well-known sporting men have taken ad
vantage of the excellent locality to take
i the place of the old resort.
Orndorff lias managed the billiard par
lors at the Cafe Royal for the past three
years and is recognized as one of the
shrewdest managers on the coast. He is
backed in the venture by George Schmidt,
well known as the proprietor of Schmidt's
Villa. ''",;
The annex has been secured, for which
the new manager* will pay $1150 per
month from the Ist of April, and will have
the place In operation In about six or
eight weeks. At present It is undergoing
extensive repairs.
O'SHHA TO PLEAD GUILTY.
Will Go to Prison for Murder in the
Second Degree.
Daniel O'Shea will appear before Judge
Dunne this morning and plead guilty to. a
charge of murder In the second degree
for having shot and killed his fiancee,
Julia Keely, who. during the last months
of her life, was employed in the Baldwin
Hotel as chambermaid. O'Shea's trial
was about to open before Judge Dunne
yesterday, when the prisoner agreed to
plead guilty to a charge of murder in
the second degree. As no one saw the
killing it is doubtful if O'Shea could have
been convicted of the first degree offense,
and as the State would be saved several
thousand dollars it was decided to permit
him to plead as stated. .v:

WILL BURN POWDER TO INCREASE RAIN.
LOS ANGELES, March 16.â di
rectors lof ' the Merchants' and
Manufacturers' Association are
yet willing to engage in an ex
periment to produce rain, not
feeling satisfied with the quantity
that has thus far been vouchsafed
in answer to the prayers made yes
terday. They have agreed to give
$500 toward paying the cost of an ex
periment on lines laid down by E. E.
Crandall.'who on" his return from San
Francisco reported to the directors
that he could get there 100,000 pounds
of powder for 5% cents a pound, and in
addition he had . obtained from the
Southern Pacific 25 per cent discount
on the high freight rate usually
charged for carrying explosives in such
bulk.
'The association is willing to risk $500
toward paying for bombarding the
heavens, and propose, if the powder be
purchased and safely brought hither,
to discharge it without the use of bal
loons or cannons. A secluded and iso
lated spot will be chosen on the low
lands, and there the 100,000 pounds of
explosive will be shot off in lots of 5000
pounds at a time. The jar is expected
to reach the rain clouds and bring to
gether elements, that will result in a
downpour. If. the first lot' brings down
such a. cloudburst as to preclude the
possibility or make unnecessary a sec
ond shot the balance of the powder will
be stacked up to remain until needed.
From every county in Southern Califor
nia comes reports of. rainfall sufficient to
save the grain crops and greatly benefit
the fruit and sugar-beet interests. The
northerly counties have had the greatest
precipitations as yet. but predictions are
for more rain and the storm is rapidly
extending southward.
Late this afternoon the barometer was
slowly but steadily falling. There Is rain
faling at Caliente, near Tehachapi, and
the Indications are that the storm is still
north of Los Angeles. The rain flag stays
up and the wind is in the right quarter.
It is believed at the Weather Bureau that
there will be a good rain to-night. The
precipitation for the storm up to 5 o'clock
was .84 of an Inch.

rains, with enow In the mountains. With

above the average.

HOW RAIN HELPS
THE PLACER MINERS
REDDING, March 16.â News of a re
markable clean-up in a placer mine on
Indian Creek. Trinity County, has just
been received In this city. A run of sixty
hours was made and $2500 in gold taken
out, or a little more than $40 an hour.
The claim is owned by Tom Bergin and
James O'Neii. Bergin is Sheriff of Trin
ity County. They have held the property
for several years, and until this- last
storm were unable to work the claim for
lark of water. The fall of a foot of snow
with subsequent heavy rains, has filled
creeks bank full and many claims that
have lain idle for two years are being
worked to their full capacity. Bergin and
O'Neil's strike has caused many locations
to be made on Indian Creek from Its
source to Its mouth.
From the activity that now prevails on
the creeks and along the Trinity River
it Is predicted that a rich harvest of gold
will certainly result. This is now the
third season since there has been any
considerable activity among the placer
miners, although the larger hydraulic
properties, with expensive ditches and
Pipe lines, have been made more or less
active. With the mountains covered with
a second coat of snow the miners of Trin
ity have reason to be enthusiastic.

STOCKTON, March 16.â The laying of

next Sunday afternoon will be the occa

Riordan and a procession of societies,
escorted by Company B, National Guard
of California, will march to the site of the
home. The institution will be a home for
old men and.will be used as a general hos
pital lor the public. Neither home nor
hospital will' be exclusively for those of
the Catholic faith,- The home will be In
charge of sisters, who will be. assisted by
nurses. All old men who are worthy and
are recommended will be taken Into the
institution. Those who are able to pay
for their care will do so; those who are
able to work will assist in the duties of
the institution and those who are neither
able to pay nor to work will be cared tor,
as the home is purely a charitable organ!-

FIVE IN KILLED
AT DOT SPRINGS
Deadly Combat Between
Sheriff and Police.
Special: Dispatch to The Call.
HOT SPRINGS, Ark., March 16.â A
shooting affray "occurred here at 5:30
o'clock this afternoon, which resulted
in the death of five men and the serious
wounding of two others, one of whom
is not expected to live. The killed are:
THOMAS TOLER, Chief of Police.
J. E. HART, city detective.
THOMAS GOSLEE, police sergeant.
JOHN -WILLIAMS, son of Sheriff Wil
liams.
LOUIS HINKLE, driver of a brewery
wagon.
Ed Spears was shot in t^he neck and
may die. .f.
The shooting was the. result of feel
ing growing out of the Mayoralty- con
test. The Sheriff was a warm sup
porter of the Democratic nominee, while
Toler, Hart and Goslee were support
ers of the opposition candidate.
Feeling ran high and early in the
afternoon phots were exchanged in
front of the City Hall between the
Sheriff and his son John on one side
and Sergeant Goslee on the other, but
no one was injured.
After this both parties determined to
have it out. Toler, Hart and Goslee
were together, walking south on Cen
tral avenue about 5:30 o'clock, when
they met Sheriff Williams and his two
sons, John and Coffey, and Ed Spears,
a deputy sheriff, in front of Lemps'
beer depot.
The quarrel was renewed. No one
can tell who fired the first shot, but in
a moment there was a general fusillade,
in which forty or fifty shots were ex
changed. When it was over Toler, Hart
and Goslee and Hinkle, a non-combat
ant, were dead, and John Williams
mortally wounded. He died "about an
hour later. Louis Hinkle attempted to
separate the combatants at the com
mencement and was shot in the head
and died instantly.
The Mayor immediately after the
shooting appointed Judge L. D. Biding
Chief of "Police, who had a posse of
deputies sworn in at once. There is lit
tle factional feeling outside of those en
gaged in the shooting. Order was easily
restored and the city is now quiet. The
Sheriff and his son Coffey are under ar
rest and no further trouble is antici
pated.
The Mayor said in an interview at 9
o'clock that the whole affair grew out
of the intense feeling on the Mayoralty
contest.
"I have ordered all saloons in the city
closed and everything is as quiet as
usual and there will be no further
trouble."
Sheriff Williams in an interview at
his home at 10 o'clock said that there
was an attempt made to assassinate
him this afternoon by a policeman on
account of his friendship for Belding,
the popular candidate for Mayor. .

GUSTAF WILL BE
FURTHER SNUBBED
Growing Feeling Against
the Crown Prince.
Special Dispatch to ] The Call.
CHRISTIANIA. . March 16.â Sixty Lei
tist members of the Storthing, the
legislative assembly .of Norway, have
declined an invitation to. dine with the
Swedish Crown, Prince Gustaf. who is
acting as regent of, Sweden and Nor
way, during the absence of King Oscar
I,' who is in Southern France, seeking
to recuperate his health.
The refusal of the Norwegian legisla
tors to accept the hospitality of the re
gent is doubly significant as emphasiz
ing the resentment provoked in Norway
by a recent saying of the Crown Prince
in a public meeting at Stockholm that
he would not object to lead the Swedes
against the headstrong Norwegians in
a resort to arms to settle . the quarrel
between the two countries, and is the
most striking indication of open en
mity to Sweden which has been shown
in Christiania of late. A few nights
ago, while the Crown Prince was re
turning from the Military Club in
Christiania, he met a crowd of people
who hooted him and pelted him with
snowballs. The Norwegian papers
condemned the demonstration, describ
ing it as in any event a purely personal
incident. But the refusal of the mem
bers of the Storthing to dine with the
regent has wider bearings and is in the
nature of a political protest against the
continuance of the union with Sweden.
In spite of the action of the Storthing
last winter in legalizing the adoption of
the separate Norwegian flag an over
whelming majority of the people of
Norway bitterly opposes the existing
regime. Of late, in both Norway and
Sweden, there has been an ominous in
crease of military and naval forces,
with clearly one object in view, to sup
port, if necessary, by arms all claims
which may lead to an entire separation
of the two countries.
The opposition to the continuance of
the union is stronger in Norway than
in Sweden, but even the Swedes con
tend that the existing union, which
rests upon the Riksakt, or Greater
Charter, declaring an irrevocable union,
without prejudice to the separate gov
ernment, constitution and laws of each
country, cannot be continued without
modifications of a radical character.
Norway's political grievance is that she
is deprived of her just share in the
common government, but the principal
grievances are of commercial origin
and provoked by the foreign trade in
terests of the two countries. Their com
mercial policies are diametrically op
posed, Sweden having adopted protec
tionism and Norway free trade.
