WORDS FAILED
TO IMPRESS HIM
Clifford Meldrum Is a
Burglar.
IGNORED MOTHER'S WARNING
CAPTURED AFTER A VERY EX
CITING CHASE.
Followed for Two Blocks by the
Woman He Had Robbed, He Is
Finally Cornered and
Arrested.
Despite the tearful appeals of his aged
mother to lead a better life. Clifford Mel
drum. alias Charles Adams, was yester
day caught in the act of burglarizing a
room in the Pearl House at Ninth and
Mission streets. His capture was brought
about by Mrs. Ashmore. the landlady of
the house, whose room he had entered.
Mrs. Ashmore wa.s seated in the kitchen
of the house when she heard a peculiar
noise as if somebody was trying to force
an entrance into her apartments, which
are directly opposite the kitchen. Rush
ing into the hallway she tried the door of
her room, but finding it locked concluded
that she was mistaken. A few minutes
later she hear-. a similar noise, and
emerging from the kitchen she encoun
tered. Meldrum in the act of leaving her
â¢ room. Accosting him she asked him what
he wanted, when he ran along the hall
way. followed by the plucky woman.
s The fleeing burglar dashed through the
entrance on Mission street and headed for
Washington avenue, which is off Mission
street, between Nil - and Tenth. Not
withstanding that Mrs. Ashmore was hat
less, she continued in pursuit of the bold
thief. After reaching Howard street
Meldrum jumped on a passing car. Mrs.
A_-hmore frantically waved her hand to
the motorman as if she desired to board
the car. The car was immediately stopped
and as the brave woman was within a
few f.et of it Meldrum jumped off and
ran down Howard street. Mrs. Ashmore
continued to low him and at the top of
her voice shout- "Stop thief!"
. Detective Crockett, who was on a pass
ing street car, seeing Meldrum running
along the street, followed by Mr.- Ash
more, joined in the pursuit. Several citi
zens also joined in the chase, and Mel
drum. realizing he could not outrun them.
entered the Hibernia Brewery and sought
to elude his pursuers by hiding in a wheat
bin in the rear of the brewery. He was
promptly discovered and taken to the
City- Prison, where he was formally
charged with burglary. A sum of money,
amounting to $13 50, which he stole from
Mrs. Ashmore's room, was found in his
pockets. The money was booked as evi
dence against him. . Y m ~y;Y.
Secreted in the lining of his coat was
discovered a pair of brass knuckles,
which the detectives believe he intended
to use if surprised by one of the male
occupants of the house.
The ' following pathetic appeal of the
devoted mother was also found on him:

Professor Charles M. 'â "'â¢">' of the Uni

resentative poet of both the Americana
and English for two reasons. First, be

race and has interpreted to the people
their own state of being. , , ?

pretation he has ascended to the position
of a leader and has pointed cut by means
of his pen their duties. He has awakened
them to a sense of duty, and it nothing
else was considered his 'Recessional and
'White Man's Burden' give finely cut

FOR THE EXPOSITION.
Building Trades Council Sends a Del
egation to Sacramento in
Labor's Interests.
The Building Trades Council last night
ratified the action of its special commit
tee, which decided last Sunday that or
ganized labor should aid the promised ex
position of 1501. It had been stated that
the workingmen were opposed to the ex
position, and this committee was ap
pointed to correct any such impression
which might now exist. The workingmen
think it is their duty, if they want help
from the exposition, to first help it along
when- their assistance is most needed.
P. H McCarthy and E. 1.. Malsbary
were delegated to go to Sacramento to
look after the interests of labor in con
nection with the exposition.
The business agent reported that the
firm of Merchant & Nicholls has the
contract for repapering Pythian Castle
and Is employing non-union men. The
matter was placed in the hands of tho
executive committee for immediate ac
tion. _

HILL AND O'BRIEN WAR
OVER PREROGATIVES
CORONER THREATENS DEPUTY
HEALTH OFFICER.
Alleges That He Has No Right to
Sign Death Certificates in
Chinatown.
A few days ago Coroner Hill went to
the plains of Merced for the purpose of
killing wild geese and jackrabbits. Be
fore he left the city he sent a communi
cation to Health Officer O'Brien, inform
ing him that he had authorized Dr. Za
bala, the autopsy surgeon, to sign death
certificates for him. To this note Dr.
O'Brien replied to the effect that no one
but a Justice of the Peace was authorized
to act in place of the Coroner and that
Dr. Zabala's certificate would not be rec
ognized by the Health Office.
The Coroner returned yesterday with
several bodies of wild ducks and geese
from the market at Merced, and was in
censed at Dr. O'Brien's action. In reply
the Coroner addressed the following note
to the Health Officer:
BAN FRANCISCO, March 16, ISM.
Dr. A. P. O'Brien, Health Officer, City and
County of San Francisco Dear Doctor: I have
just received your letter, dated March 13, in
which you say: "The State law speciflcally
states that during the absence of the Coroner
a Justice of the Peace must perform these
duties."
in the same communication you object to Dr.
Zabala countersigning death certificates re
quiring the attention of your office. This is a
point lhat never has been raised to my knowl
edge, and your department under the prevl.us
administration of this offlce accepted counter
signs by the Coroner's deputies.
I find, however, or* looking up the law. that
you are right and that nobody but the Coroner
'. r a Justice of the Peace, in his absence, has
the right to certify to a death that has oc
curred from any other cau- than . a sickness
that has been attended by a physician. This
being the case lt becomes my duty to notify
you that one of yov subordinates. Dr. Mc-
Murdo, has been for some time past signing
death certificates In direct violation of the
law which you quote.
I must, therefore, ask you to issue an order
forbidding him to do so in the future. From
and after this date any cases of this kind must
be certified to or countersigned by me as Coro
ner in conformity with the law.
I am very sorry to deprive Dr. McMurdo of
the tees he was worit to receive for doing this
work, but you will agree with me that the law
is paramount.
Thanking you for calling my attention to this
law. which I had overlooked, and trusting that
you will see to it that the law will be obeyed
in this 'egard in the future, and that you will
notify the employes of the Health Department
of my request in the matter, I remain youre
very respectfully, E. E. HILL, M. D.
Coroner.
Dr. McMurdo was appointed about four
years ago to look after the deaths in the
House of Correction and the Old Indus
trial School building. These buildings
have since passed out of his jurisdiction,
and he has been signing death certificates
for the indigent and Chinese, receiving
fees which mount up to a respectable sum
at the end of the month. He was appoint
ed by the Board of. Health and is not
subject to the Coroner in any way. The
Coroner's action in the matter may give
rise to some litigation which will deter
mine and define the rights, duties and
privileges of his office.

THE SAN FB Ay CISCO CALL, FRIDAY, MABCH 17, 1899.
ENDED HIS TROUBLES.
James Finnerty, After Making Deliberate
Preparations, Shot Himself.
JAMES FINNERTY. who lodged with
Mrs. Brady at 813 Mission street, shot
himself in the right temple yesterday
afternoon. Death was instantaneous.
Mrs. Brady is at a loss to account for
the action of her lodger. She says that
the last time she saw him alive was at
about 2 o'clock yesterday afternoon. He
came into the kitchen with a bundle con
taining his week's laundry under his arm,
joked with her pleasantly for a few
minutes and passed upstairs to his room.
About two hours later Mrs. Brady sent
her little daughter upstairs to fill the
lamps. The child pushed the door of
Finnerty's room open and was horrified to
see blood on the floor. She rushed down
stairs and informed her mother, who at
once summoned a policeman. Officer
Frailer responded. He entered the room
and found Finnerty lying on the bed with
a pistol beside him. He was fully dressed
and had on a clean white shirt, white col
lar and white necktie. Before shooting
himself he had turned down the white
spread so that it would not be soiled with
his blood.
Finnerty was about 38 years of age and
a bag maker by occupation. He lost his
position last September and since that
time has been drinking heavily. He was
also subject to epileptic fits and was re
NEW STEAMER
BUILDING FOR
COAST TRADE
To Be the Largest of
Her Class.
OVERDUE SHIP REACHES PORT
GAMBLERS WITH RISKS ON THE
ALCIDES HAPPY.
Battleship lowa to Be Repaired at
the Union. Iron Works â
er Starbuck a Total
Loss.
A splendid steamer for the coast trade
is now being built on Ppget Sound for
the J. S. Kimball Company. She will be
235 feet long. 38 feet broad and 16 feet
draft. She will be engined In San Fran
cisco, and is expected to make fifteen
knots on her trial, but on ordinary occa-
sions twelve knots will be her speed. Her
carrying capacity is to be 1500 tons dead
weight, and she will have first-class ac
commodations for 100 passengers. The
new boat will be sixty feet longer than
the Dirigo and the largest and finest
steam schooner ever built on the coast
An idea of the vessel's dimensions can be
gained from the fact that she will be ten
feet longer and 4 feet 7 inches broader
than the Pacific Coast Steamship Com
pany's Pomona.
J. S. Kimball says the vessel will be
ready about next October. She will be
loaded -with lumber on the Sound and
towed to San Francisco. Her machinery
will then be put in and her deckhouses
built, after which she will at once go Into
commission.

fair Captain Young decided to put to
sea. The tug Relief towed him out past
the lightship, and the vessel then spread
all sail and went scudding up the coast.
The American ship William H. Smith for
New York and the Italian ship Cavaliere
Ciampa for Genoa, Italy, were also to
have gone to sea, but the captains de
cided to remain in port and wait for a
change of wind.
The United States collier Brutus went
to Mare Island yesterday. She will be
overhauled and will then load coal for
Guam. The work of loading the trans
port Cleveland is being rushed, and it is
expected to get the vessel ready for sea
by to-morrow. The City of Puebla has
, been cleaned up and will be ready to sail
as soon as the troops are aboard, while
the men on the Zealandia are working
night and 'day in order to have the trans
port go out next week.
Felix Reid was badly hurt on the Brit
ish ship General Roberts at Union-street
wharf yesterday. He missed his footing
and fell down the hold. His left ear was
nearly torn off and his eye was badly cut,
his scalp torn and his hip bruised. He
will be laid up for a couple of weeks.
Joseph Ambrose, a quartermaster on
the steamer George W. Elder, Is supposed
to have fallen overboard last Monday and
been drowned. He was on duty that
night and when his relief came he was
nowhere to be found. The George W. El
der was on her way from Portland to San
Francisco at the time.

the Toyo Kisen Kaisha was thrown open
for the recaption of the public yesterday
afternoon from 3 to 9:30 p. m. It was the
ship's busy time, as she sails to-day for
the Asiatic ports, and the steam winches
were hoisting in tons of cargo, but the
courteous Captain Filmer. assisted by
Surgeon Brimacombe of the vessel, re
ceived the guests and conducted them
over the steamer.
The visitors, especially the ladies, were
loud In their praise of the elegant fittings
of the beautiful saloons and staterooms.
Nor was such praise amiss, for the Hong
kong Maru Is the richest furnished mail
steamer that has ever entered this har
bor her cost in England being jfoO.OOO.
In the grand saloon tea wa3 served each
guest by Captain Filmer and Dr. Brima
combe. * The greater number of the visi
tors went * aboard in the evening and
viewed the white ship's interior when the
electric lights were glowing in every
apartment. *:*'t?-yY'f , Â
The officers of the Hongkong Maru
are" Captain W. E. Filmer, Chief Officer
W. Smith. Second Officer R. Davies,
Third Officer K. Shirine, Fourth Officer P.
Ota Surgeon R. Brimacombe, Purser
John Darby, Chief Engineer W. Wilkins.
Chief Steward E. Smith, Second Engineer
J Crompton, Third Engineer W. Johnson,
Fourth Engineer F. Hardy.
The Hongkong Maru, like her two sis
ter* Nippon Maru and America Maru,
were lately built in England by Sir James
Lalng "Maru" means merchant, to dis
tinguish those vessels from ships of
war "Toyo Risen Kaisha" is Oriental
<v>mpanv, and that is the English for the
name of the firm that runs the three
splendid white steamers across the Pa
cific. Their house-flag Is an open fan
adorned with the red sun of Japan, all
on a white field.
The Pacific Exposition.
Three weeks ago a letter of inquiry was
sent to the members of the Merchants'
Association to ascertain their views upon
the proposed exposition in 1901. A return
postal was enclosed with the following
question. "Do you favor the proposed Pa
cific Ocean Exposition in 1901?"
Secretary Freud yesterday ,said: "Up
to date we have received 394 responses, of
which 157 replies are decidedly In favor of
the proposed exposition; twenty-nine are
favorable upon certain conditions, while
195 answers are decidedly opposed to the
exposition, and thirteen are conditionally
opposed to It."
SUDDEN DEATH OF
JOSEPH MEDILL
The Veteran Editor of the Chicago
Tribuoe Ca!rr)iy Says at tbe
Close "Wbat Is the News?"
SAX ANTONIO. Texas, March 16.â Joseph Medill, editor of the Chicago
Tribune, died here this morning at 10:30 o'clock of heart failure. He
was conscious up to the moment of death, which he met with calm
placidity. He said to his private physician ten minutes before he died:
"My last words shall be 'What is the news?'"
He came here three months ago for his health, and had been confined
to his bed only five days. His grandson, Robert R. McCormick, was with
him in his last moments. His remains will be sent to Chicago at once.
Mr. Medill was born in* New Brunswick in 1523. When he was nine
years of age his father moved to Ohio, where the son worked on a farm
while acquiring an education. He subsequently studied law and practiced
for some time at Massillon. In 1549 he founded a free soil newspaper in
Coshocton, and in 1552 established a Whig newspaper in Cleveland. In 1854
he became one of the organizers of the Republican party, and since that
time has been prominent in its councils. He removed to Chicago, and in
1355 became one of the owners of the Tribune. He was a member of the
Illinois Constitutional Convention of 1870 and the author of the minority
representation clause, and in ISTI was a member of the United States Civil
Service Commission. He was elected Mayor of Chicago in the same year
and later spent some time in Europe. On his return he purchased a con
trolling interest in the Tribune and had since been its editor-in-chief.
WENT OVER A GRADE.
Serious Buggy Accident oh a Moun-
tain Road Near Santa Cruz.
SANTA CRUZ. March 16.â accident
occurred on a mountain road several days
ago which resulted in the death \ of a
horse and the serious injury of . a young
lady. Miss Lockhart and Miss Gunther
were driving along the grade near Felton.
The horse became frightened at some cat
tle on the road and backed over the
grade and fell down the embankment fifty
feet. The buggy was smashed and the
horse was instantly killed. Miss Lockhart
jumped from the buggy, but Miss Gunther
had a narrow escape from death and was
1 saved by a clump of bushes which kept
I her from going the fifty feet. She was
injured seriously about the shoulder and
â  left leg and is under a doctor's care.

THE CRIME AGAINST THE ARMY.
WHEN the Court of Inquiry was appointed to
investigate the charges made by General Miles
against the meat supplies furnished the army at
Tampa and at Santiago it is probable the beef Con
tractors and such allies- as they had in the War De
partment thought they could disprove the statements
of the General and the reports of his officers, or at
least convince the public that the amount of bad meat
was so small in proportion to the whole quantity sup
plied that they would be virtually relieved of the
odium put upon them. If they had such hopes they
are now the most disappointed men in the United
States.
Not only have all the charges of General Miles
been sustained, but facts even blacker than those
stated in his reports have been brought to light. One
of the witnesses, Lieutenant Davis of the First Cav
alry, swore before the court that he had found mag
gots in the canned beef. When asked how they got into
â the meat he replied they were canned there, and when
further asked if he did not know that in the process
of canning the meat undergoes such heat that no
animal life could survive in it, he retorted, "I did not
say they were alive. They were dead."
That testimony means that beef already rotten and
swarming with maggots was in that condition canned
and furnished to the army for food. The revelation of
this fact puts an end to the specious plea of the con
tractors that the beef was spoiled by the heat of the
climate in Southern Florida and Cuba. It proves that
the meat was rotten before it was packed at Chicago,
and that the fraud was deliberately perpetrated re
gardless of the consequences to the soldiers who were
to be fed with it.
For an offense of that kind the punishment should
be prompt and condign. The crime is almost as great
and as mean as that of intentional poisoning, and no
man guilty of committing it or of conniving at it
should be permitted to go unwhipped of justice.
CRUELTY TO f.NIMALS.
IN the list of crimes there is not one' more con
temptible than that of cruelty to animals. Some
times a poor man, earning a living by teaming,
seems almost forced to work horses while they are
unfit, but even for such a man the law makes no ex
ception. If caught, he is punished.
A person unable to give his horse food and shelter
has no business to have a horse. For the person who
owns many horses and permits them all to suffer, un
stabled and unfed, there is not the ghost of palliation.
He should be punished to the limit of the law, and,
as the law does not provide for confiscation of the
property, infliction of a fine and a term in prison, it
is inadequate. Still, such as it is, it should be applied
with the utmost vigor.
Horseman McCarty is accused, and not for the
first time, of having allowed a band of horses to so
nearly starve to death that their pitiful condition at
tracted the attention of passers-by, so that complaint
was made, and an arrest followed. McCarty ought
to plead guilty and promise to reform. He had re
cently bought these horses, and if he knew he would
be unable to care for them the act of buying them was
in itself a wrong. He might have bought fewer, horses
and devoted some of his capital to the purchase of
feed.
It is to be hoped that McCarty will get such a
dose of justice this time as shall tend not only to re
form him, but to constitute of him a terrible example
to warn others addicted to similarly brutal practices.
If we invest ten millions in cleaning Havana, and,
through a syndicate, lend from twelve to twenty mil
lions to the Cubans, the world may soon witness an
extension of the principles of the Declaration of In
dependence through the simple operation of fore
closure. This will be an improvement on expansion
by bombardment.
New York authorities are about to subject a woman
to electrocution. There is a limit to gallantry. The
only mitigating circumstance about the case is the
sex of the prisoner, and this is offset largely by the
fact that a lot of cackling old hens are demanding of
the Governor that he commute the sentence.
When people pray for rain and get hail they have no
great reason- to complain. Still, if dissatisfied with
what is sent, they would be wise next time to pray
for hail.
Perhaps the people of this country are callous, but
they draw the line considerably this side of feeding
â¢ soldiers on meat and maggots.
Another mob in Georgia has covered the State with
disgrace. There was no occasion.
THE END OF THE SESSION.
THE session of . the Legislature is drawing to a
close.. It has been a successful session in many
respects. The inaugural admonition of the Gov
ernor touching the lucidity and simplicity of statutes
had its effect, and the Governor has been at pains to
aid the Legislature in the direction of his very wise
and pertinent suggestion. Up to this stage economy
in expenditures has not been lost sight of, and we are
persuaded that a proper oversight of boards of control
of the State institutions will enable the covering back
into the treasury of considerable sums which it has
been considered expedient to appropriate.
Much of the paternal legislation proposed by some
of the members has met its fate, and what remains. of
such propositions will probably fail to become law.
The session will go to a respectable position in the
legislative history of the State.
In one respect it has so far been remarkable in a
most respectable and praiseworthy direction, in re
fusing to make an improper selection for United
States Senator. The Republican majority is respon
sible for the acts and the place in history of this Leg
islature. That majority and a Republican Governor
have done well by the people in legislation. And that
majority has done still better by the people in its re
fusal to sadden the spirit and tarnish the fame of the
State by an election of Senator that cannot be justi
fied on the very highest grounds. The Legislatu-c
might have earned even more credit as a law-making
body, and yet' lost it all and the credit of the '.Repub
lican party -too by a defiance of public opinion and
public decency in the election of Burns.
If the majority leave the seat vacant it will be a clean
vacancy, and the best and most upright judgment in
the State will approve it. Then the party represented
by the majority will get the full credit and lasting
benefit of the good legislation passed and the useful
economies effected, and will go into the battle royal
of fooo under a banner as stainless as a star.
Every Republican is inspired by the prospect of
marching to that fight with no incubus on the party,
no smirch on its reputation, no folly to explain, no
offense to deny or admit. Every one feels the thrill
of a coming campaign which no ingenuity can turn
into a defense, but which will be an attack from the
sounding of the long roll to the shouting that an
nounces victory. The election of Burns would have
made the campaign defensive and explanatory,
apologetic and evasive from the start. If it had taken
place what answer could any supporter of Burns make
to the enemy's charge of bad faith, promises forgot
ten, pledges broken and professions hollow and de
ceitful? ,
Where in the peculiar ranks of the Burns following
is the genius to answer what all men would assert if
he were made Senator? As for those who last year
asserted the high purpose, pure aims, exalted mis
sion and solemn faith of the party, and did it out of
sincere conviction, being deceived and made false by
the election of Burns, how could they be expected to
revamp broken pledges and make vestal again the
party purity, destroyed by such an abuse of its power
and misuse of its majority?
The members of the majority in the Legislature de
sire that their work shall be justified of the people
and that it shall be vindicated by the election of a
Republican' succession. The Governor does not wish
the calamity to him and the thwarting of his ideals
that would be involved in the election of an adverse
Legislature in the middle of his term.
All these evils that frown above the political hori
zon of the future will sink below it if the Republican
members stand by their set purpose to leave a clean
â vacancy in the Senate rather than defile the seat.
SUPREME COURT REFORMS.
IT is to be hoped that some means of relieving the
Supreme Court may be found without exposing
the taxpayers of the State to the expense of main
taining three Courts of Appeal and to the danger of
having additional appellate tribunals established
whenever the Legislature may desire to create places
for judicial aspirants. It is also desirable that sim
plicity and conciseness in legal records be secured, in
conformity with suggestions already made by The
Call.
These reforms, however, are especially related to
this State. There is a deeper and transcendently im
portant question bearing upon the practice and upon
the administration of the law that affects every State
and Territory in the Union and the solution of which
cannot be long postponed. Legal publications, in
cluding reports, digests, textbooks and unclassified
compilations, have become so numerous and many of
them are so worthless that, even though the publica
tion mania should be checked, it is doubtful whether
the efficiency of the bar and of the bench has not al
ready been impaired to a degree that calls for radical
treatment.
There are forty-five States in the Union, some of
which have several Courts of Appeal, in addition to
hundreds of courts of original jurisdiction, general,
special and limited, that cover the entire range of law
and equity. The Territories are proportionately sup
plied. In addition to the Supreme Court of the
United States and the Court of Claims, the law busi
ness within the Federal jurisdiction is transacted by
Circuit Courts of Appeal, by Circuit Courts, by Dis
trict Courts and by Commissioners, so that the legiti
mate Federal contributions to the literature of the law
are alone beyond the digestive or assimilating power
, .of the greatest mind or the greatest memory that the
1 bar or the judiciary can produce.
But owing to the laxness with which the matricula
tion of aspirants for professional life is permitted all
over the United States, and excessively in California,
the average quality of the bar and of the bench has
been lowered, and the reports, except those of some
of the higher courts, among which our own Supreme
Court is embraced, are replete with loose, prolix and
conflicting opinions. There is hardly a petty question
of practice that can arise upon either side of which a
mass of authority, so called, cannot be arrayed, and
in these days arguments and briefs, other than those
proceeding from a select few, are largely guesses at
the law, fortified by innumerable and flabby citations.
In addition to the reports, which in quantity and in
quality are daily invitations to apoplectic repletion,
digests are published in almost every. State, and pre
texts for legal pot-boiling are daily inventions.
There is a perpetual deluge of this stuff. There is not
a heading in any of the elementary treatises, such as
the Commentaries of Blackstone and Kent, the works
of Story, Parsons, Greenleaf, Bishop and other law
writers of equal eminence, which is not in these days
made the excuse for separate publications. The
Parisian fashions are changed four times a year and
original, artistic skill consumed to the point of ex
haustion in order to drain the pockets of rich and
complaisant husbands and fathers throughout the
world. Law publishers have long since accepted this
example, and with new books and with pew editions
of old books lawyers who endeavor to maintain li
braries are sucked so dry that they are scarcely able
to live, and indigestible, weak and swashy matter ac
cumulates on their hands until their minds are as
gorged and congested as their purses are empty.
There are those who believe that the period has
THE SAN FRANCISCO CAI_I_, FRIDAY, MARCH 17, 1899.
almost arrived when instructed' and equipped lawyers
and Judges, imbued with the essential principles of
applied justice and with their reasoning faculties fully
developed, will have to present and 'determine every
case on its intrinsic merits, and when the use of
authority will practically cease. This is one of the
radical changes in the administration of the law that
is probably inevitable.
JOSEPH H. CHOATE has long been esteemed
one of the foremost forensic orators of New
York and one, of its wittiest after-dinner speak
ers. â  As a pleader he is perhaps without an equal at the
New York bar, and at the banquet board his only
rival is Chauncey Depew. His appointment as Em
bassador to the court of St. James has given him an
opportunity to shine in London, and he has taken ad
vantage of the first banquet given in his honor to
dazzle the Britishers.
The speech was witty and genial and well suited to
the audience. It was received with manifestations of
rapturous pleasure, the orator was repeatedly cheered,
and we are told that at its close he took his seat
"amid perfect thunders of applause." So far as the
immediate effect is concerned, therefore, the Embas
sador achieved a brilliant success, and his debut in
diplomacy was a genuine triumph.
Unfortunately the wit of the address does not stand
the test of translation from spoken words to a printed
report. Americans reading the speech far away from
the glow of the lights, the sound of the music, the
odor of flowers, the sumptuous surroundings of the
rich banquet hall, and hearing nothing of the sonorous
voice of the orator, nor catching any light from his
expressive countenance, will not be so well pleased
with the speech as were the British banqueters.
In truth Mr. Choate's address is another evidence
that what in a private citizen may pass as a pleasant
wit will, when coming from an Embassador, sound
like a foolish word. A diplomatist charged with the
duty of representing his country abroad cannot be
allowed the freedom permitted to an eloquent gen
tleman talking to his own people and familiar friends
over the walnuts and the wine at home. When he
takes that liberty his most genial sallies become his
greatest blunders, because hardly any blunder is ever
worse than that of a misplaced wit.
An illustration of Mr. Choate's mistake in this re
spect is to be found in his reference to the Venezue
lan controversy between this country and Great
Britain. On that subject our Embassador is reported
to have said to the applauding banqueters: "You
know on our side of the water we love occasionally
to twist the lion's tail for the mere sport of hearing
him roar. Well, that time he disappointed us. He
would not roar at all. He sat silent as the Sphinx,
and by dint of mutual forbearance, our sober second
thought aiding your sober first thought, we avoided
everything but a mere' war of words."
It would be difficult to conceive a more objection
able statement than that on the part of an American
Embassador. Even Bayard never did worse. The
part taken by the American Government in the
Venezuelan affair was not designed to twist the lion's
tail to make him roar, but to uphold the Monroe doc
trine, one of the cardinal policies of the republic to
which our people are most devoted. Nor is it cor
rect to say our sober second thought on the issue
accorded with the sober first thought of Great Britain.
We had no second thought on the question at all.
The statements and even the tone of the Venezuelan
message are as firmly approved to-day as when the
document was first made public.
Of course all understand the Embassador was
joking. He was making a free and easy after-dinner
speech along lines to which he has been accustomed.
It was a success in that respect, for it set the table in
a roar, but Mr. Choate is likely to be surprised by an
answering roar from this country which will not be
so pleasing to his ears, and tl^en he will learn there
is no petard more dangerous to a diplomatist than a
misplaced wit.
A PARABLE FROM MAINE.
DOWN in the State of Maine there is a member
of the Legislature of this year who was once
a Justice of the Peace, and who is known as
Judge McFadden. Notwithstanding he is in the
Legislature, he is a statesman; and, notwithstanding
he was once a Justice of the Peace, he is judicious as
well as judicial. Moreover, he is a Christian â in
some respects at leastâ for he teaches in parables.
Judge McFadden has observed that for many years
past it has been the custom of philanthropical so
cieties, benevolent institutions and charitable asso
ciations of all kinds to apply to the State for money
to aid them in their good work. He has also noted
that the money is always given, and has come to the
conclusion that the State treasury is being used as a
soft snap by every kind of folks who wish to make a
reputation as benefactors. Under such convictions
the Judge addressed to his fellow legislators this
parable:
"When our forefathers, nearly a century ago, suc
ceeded in divorcing the old man State from the ven
erable Mrs. Church, I think they intended that he
should live forever after in celibacy. I think they did
not intend that he should get entangled with any
females thereafter. But, sir, there has of late years
grown up a remarkable flirtation between the old man
State and the beautiful, the young that is, compared
with Mrs. Church â and the very amiable Miss Char
ity. Now, I have not anything to say against the
woman. She is not a bit like the old lady
Church. She is not dogmatic, she is not intolerant;
she is very agreeable: she is altogether lovely, but
she has one fault â is an awful spendthrift. She
wheedles all the money she can out of the old man,
and she doesn't seem to care where he gets it. If
only he can hand it over to her for the objects that
are dear to her heart and close to her pride she
doesn't ask a question as to its source. Sometimes I
think that she would be willing for him to commit
highway robbery if he would only hand over to her
the sums she asks for."
Some people think this parable wise, and some
think it absurd, but all who hear it think about it one
way or another. In the East it is being discussed al
most as extensively as a speech by the President, and,
judging from reports that come to us, the prevailing
opinion is that benevolent societies ought to prac
tice benevolence at their own expense, to some ex
tent, at any rate, and that the moral of the parable
is applicable to more States than Maine.
'The man and woman found sleeping deeply under
the benign influence of gas may be glad that they re
covered, but there are times when publicity has its
disadvantages.
Builders are not to be permitted hereafter to
blockade the walks. That pedestrians have rights
will be a genuine surprise to them.
We arc waiting to hear some Sacramento editor
exclaim, "Thank God, the Legislature has adjourned."
According to the mortuary reports from Manila
there will soon be left no Filipinos to shoot at.

SUPERVISORS
GRANT RELIEF
TO SHIPPERS
Extra Men for Fourth-
Street Bridge.
TO AID PASSAGE OF BOATS
TROLLEYS MAY FORM A NET
WORK OVER TOWN.
Representatives of Merchants' Asso.
ciation and Improvement Clubs
Appear Before the Street
Committee.
The Street Committee of the Super
visors did not require much time at its
meeting yesterday to find a way out of
the difficulty regarding the opening and
closing of Fourth-street bridge. This aid
to navigation is under the control of the
Supervisors, and ship-owners have pro
tested strongly against what they claim
is an injustice to them in the operation
of the bridge. This is a charge made by
the bridge tenders for opening that struc
ture for the passage of a vessel after
dark. The captains of coasting schooners
must go through the bridge when the tide
serves, and object to the extra charge,
which becomes considerable in the course
of a month.
The matter came up late ln the after
noon, and Chairman Aigeltlnger at once
recommended that two additional men be
employed to take charge of the bridge at
night. He did not think the board should
stand In the way of the shipping inter
ests. The remaining members of the com
mittee agreed with him. The committee
will report favorably on the matter at the
meeting of the board on Monday, when
the desired relief will, without doubt, be
granted the long-suffering shipping men.
The Merchants' Association swooped
down upon the committee during the
afternoon, and for over an hour Indulged
in rhetoric and extemporaneous- elo
quence. The theme was the old one, the
granting of franchises to the Market
street Railway Company for the new lines
on Grant avenue from Geary to Bush
street and thence east to Sansome, and
on Taylor, between Geary and Post. Their
efforts were chiefly directed, however,
toward the establishment of an electric
system over the following streets:
Sixth street from Brannan to Market,
thence across Market to Taylor, thence
along Taylor to Geary, to Grant avenue;
Montgomery from Market to Jackson,
thence to Sansome and along Sansome to
Market street; Washington street from
Montgomery to Sansome street; Post
street from Market to Leavenworth
street, to City Hall avenue, to Grove, to
Polk, to Market, across to Tenth and out
Tenth to Bryant street.
The second petition, if granted, will
give the Market Street Railway Com
pany franchises on Grant avenue from
Geary to Bush and thence eastwardly to
Sansome street, connecting with the net
work of trolleys and tracks provided lor
in the first ordinance.
The third petition is for the granting of
a franchise to the Market Street Railway
Company for a street railroad on Taylor
between Geary and Post.
The Sixth Street Improvement Associa
tion and the North Central Improvement
Association came into the Street Com
mittee's sanctum hand in hand with the
members of the Merchants' Association.
The petitioners took their places against
the wall and President Dohrmann set the
hall roiling. He spoke of the necessity
for rapid transit anil pictured the slow
-moving horse car traveling along Mont
gomery and Sansome streets, a menace
to business ami the eye-sore' of property
owners. In closing, President Dohrmann
prayed for the realization of the desires
of the petitioners and that the Market
Street Railway Company be granted the
franchises at once.
The present petition of the association
and improvement clubs differs from the
preceding petitions on the same matter
only as to the time limits for the com
mencement and completion of the work
by the Market-street Railway Company.
Instead of one year for the beginning and
three years for the completion of the
work the new petition fixes a time limit
of six months for the eomencement and
one. year for the completion.
General Warfield, A. Sbarboro, H. H.
Taylor, Lipman Sachs, Joseph Donahue,
David Rich and others addressed the
committee and indorsed the petition.
All sorts of improvements were prom
ised by the speakers. They said the
merchants on Montgomery street would
pay half the expense of bituminizing that
thoroughfare from Market street to
Jackson. The Supervisors presentâKal
ben. Collins. Aigeltinger, Attrtdge and
Hollandâ listened attentively, and finally
decided to lay the matter over for two
weeks.
It is hardly likely that the committee
will recommend that the petition of the
association be granted unless the route
be changed. The opposition to the fran
chises is that the new electric lines will
cut too many street corners where traffic
is extensive.
The communication received from the
Health Department and the Superintend
ent of Streets in regard to the abatement
of cesspool nuisances on the grounds of
the Affiliated Colleges, were considered,
and a resolution was passed empowering
the City Engineer to prepare plans for a
sewer system for the district bounded by
First. Seventh and Parnassus avenues
and II street. __.

''Scorching" on Bicycles.
In response to a letter from Paul M.
Nippert, president of the Associated Cycle
Clubs, complaining of the non-enforce
ment of the ordinance passed about three
years ago for regulating the use of bi
cycles, bicycle tandems, vehicles and ma
chines of a similar character. Chief Lees
yesterday sent written orders to the cap
tains of all districts in the city instruct
ing them to see that the ordinance was
strictly observed, particularly in regard
to "scorching." Every bicyclist violating
the ordinance will be promptly arrested.

NOBLE WORKERS AND
GREAT ACHIEVEMENTS
EMANU-EL SISTERHOOD ELECTS
ITS OFEICEKS.
The Society. in a Prosperous Condi
tion, With a Brilliant Record
of Good Deeds.
The fifth annual meeting and election
of officers of the Emanu-El Sisterhood
was held yesterday afternoon in the
vestry room of the Temple Emanu-El on
Sutter street. Preliminary to the election
the various committees submitted thellr
reports, showing that the charitable
organization has a noble record of good
deeds accomplished, with no indebtedness
and $2161 in bank to its credit.
The various branches of the club, tho
sewing class, the employment bureau, the
relief committee, the kindergarten and
the mothers' club are all noted in pros
perous condition and extending their
sphere of usefulness.
The latest adjunct of the organization,
the young men's club, known as the Mac
cabeeans, under the direct supervision of
Rev. Dr. Voorsanger and Rev. Dr. Nieto,
was reported in a flourishing condition,
with an active membership of sixty, the
result of two months' organization. At
present the ladies of the Sisterhood meet
at 1310 Folsom street, but the extended
scope of the charity coupled with the
rapid growth of the organization will
shortly make larger headquarters an ab
solute necessity. The pet scheme of the
ladies is to have a club house of their
own, and with this end in view an enter
tainment to start a nucleus for the build
ing will be held early in April in the mag
nificent Concordia Club rooms.
There was only one ticket- in the field.
and the following candidates were elected
without a dissenting vote:
Honorary president. Dr. Voorsanger; presi
dent, Mrs. P. N. Lilienthal; vice presidents,
Mrs. J. Lowenberg. Mrs. M. Esberg. Mrs. J.
Voorpanger; treasurer, Mrs. Louis Gerstle; re
cording secretary, Mrs. J. M. Rothchlld; cor
responding secretary. Miss Victoria Lilienthal;
council of administrationâ Mrs. Charles Col
man, Mrs. J. H. Neustadter, Mrs. A. Brown,
Mrs. Leon Sloes, Mrs. A. A. Adler, Mrs.
Charles Baum, .Mrs. I. W. Hellman, Mrs.
William Haas, Mrs. J. S. Ackerman, Mrs. J.
K. Davidson, Mrs. B. Anspacher, Mrs. D.
Elkes, Mrs. H. Wangenhelm, Mrs. B. Drey,
fus, Mrs. R. Kaufman.
FIGHTERS EOK. MANILA.
The Ninth Infantry Is Expected Here
Some Time Next Week.
In line with the offensive campaign re
cently inaugurated by General Otis
against the insurgents it is the purpose of
the "War Department co send all the avail
able regular troops to the Philippines and
place them at the disposal of General
('tis. The Ninth Infantry is expected here
next week en route to Manila. It will be
closely followed by the Twenty-flrst In
fantry and a portion of the Sixth Artil
lery.
Lieutenant George A. Deutchmendy,
First Infantry, has been ordered to Sacra
mento for recruiting duty.
First Lieutenant Cecil Stewart, Fourth
Cavalry, has been. relieved from tempora
ry duty at the Presidio and detailed to act
as quartermaster and commissary on
the transport City of Puebla.
Troops B and fiX, Fourth Cavalry, now
stationed at the Presidio, will leave some
time in April for the National Parks to
do police duty. Their places will prob
ably be supplied by a part of the Twen
ty-fourth Infantry. This regiment is
composed of negroes, but is commanded
by white officers.

States navy is 27.6 tons; 12-inch guns, 45.2
tons; 13-inch guns, 60.5 tons. There are no
80-ton guns in the naval ordnance of the
United States.

CAN AN INDIAN VOTE?â A., French
Camp, Cal. This correspondent asks:
"Can a native Indian vote in California?"'
There is nothing in the first section of
article one of the constitution of the State
of California, which Is on the right of
suffrage, which says that a native Cali
fornia Indian shall not have the right of
suffrage, but, like any other voter, he
must have the qualifications that are laid
down in that section. In Mendocino Coun
ty, where there are many Indians, the
rule Is that full-blood Indians owning land
and half-breeds are allowed to vote.

Th e id professor has a history . His real
name is Charles J. Callan, and in 1888 he
kept a saloon on Pacific street. He got
himself into trouble that year by enter
ing the saloon on Stockton street kept
hy Bill Price, the colored pugilist, and
flinging a whisky glass in Prices face
The glass struck Price on the eye and
destroyed the optic. Callan was arrested
and on November 26 of that year got two
years in San Quentin for assault with a
deadly weapon. He was released July 26,
During his Incarceration Callan's hand
some face and figure attracted the atten
tion of Mrs. Higby, the wealthy widow of
an ex-Congressman, who lived in Oak
land with her two daughters. She was
deeply interested in the reform of crimi
nals and took a special interest in Callan.
She kept a home in Oakland for reformed
criminals and after his release Callan
went to live at the home. The widow be
came infatuated with him and they were
married. Trouble soon followed, as Cal
lan did not treat his aged wife with the
consideration due her station. In fact,
it was alleged that he was in the habit
of beating her and an application for
divorce followed. Callan fought the di
vorce and the proceedings caused quite
a sensation at the time, as there was con
siderable property involved. The wife
and her daughters were successful and
Callan went in search of fresh widows to
conquer. '. _..
Callan denied his identity in the City
Prison after his surrender, but he was
recognized by several people who knew
him and spoke to him. He has now
shaved off his whiskers and looks hand
somer than ever. -.. f_
Cruelty to His Horses.
W. T. Hooper of the Society for the
Prevention of Cruelty to Animals "swore
to a complaint yesterday in Judge Gra
ham's court for the arrest of "White
Hat" McCarty on the charge of cruelty to
animals. McCarty is out of the city and
the warrant could not be served yester
day. The fourteen horses taken from
Seventh avenue and B street Wednesday
to the pound were handed over yesterday
to McCartv's foreman. The animals were
not impounded, but taken to the pound
to be properly cared for and fed. Mc-
Carthys son declares that the -horses
were sufficiently fed and that his father
can prove the allegation.
Custom House Boilers.
Collector Jackson was notified yester
day by the Secretary of the Treasury that
George H. Tay & Co. of this city had
been awarded the contract to furnish
new boilers in the Appraisers' building
for working the elevators and other pur
poses. The contract price Is $8000, and
the boilers must be furnished within 120
days from the date of the approval of
the bonds.

Every bee carries his market basket
round his hind legs. Any one examining
tiie body of a bee through a microscope
will observe that on the hind legs of the
creature there is a frjnge of stiff hairs
en tbe surface, the hairs approaching
each other at the tips, so as to form a
sort of cage. This is the bee's basket,
and into it, after a successful journey, he
will cram enough pollen to last him for
two or three days.

GAMBLING AT
INGLESIDE IS
ALL BUT BEAD
Police Make Another
Big Raid.
VAIN PLEA TO BOOKMAKERS
CRAFTY PLOT OF THE RACING
MANAGERS.
They Hire Track Followers to Submit
to Arrest The Fight to Be
Vigorously Prosecuted
in the Courts.
The struggle of Ed Corrigan and his
associate gamblers to debase, by their
continued existence, the people of this
city, will now be carried into the
courts of the State. The latter, as far
as the legislative and Police Depart
ment of San Francisco have power to
win, has been won. Bookmaking and
poolselling, the twin terrors of Ingle
side, are dead. Only the action of a
court can bring them back to life, and
the gamblers are preparing to nake
the effort. The ordinance adopted by
the Supervisors and signed by the
Mayor has-been enforced as far as the
vigilance of Chief of Police Lees and
his subordinates can do so.
Public gambling on horseraces in this
city has been stopped. Twelve more
arrests were made yesterday at Ingle
side, but the men who were taken into
custody violated the law simply to as
sist the officers of the Pacific Coast
Jockey Club to show their contempt
for the government and laws of the
city. There was no open gambling at
the track. Bookmakers declined to ply
their trade, although the representa
tives of the association did everything
to induce the gamblers to go into their
booths. The bookmakers begged to be
excuse*], notwithstanding the assurance
that they would be defended In court
by the association.
Fortunately for the city and its peo
ple Ingleside is now a wreck of itself.
There was a small crowd, not more
than 200 people, at the track. The
spectators were disgusted and the
. horsemen despondent. The races were
run without interest, and what wagers j
that were made upon them were made j
secretly. The great evil of the track â 
was dead, and the day's meeting had j
hardly the dignity of a formality.
Shortly before the first race Chief j
Legs and a corps of fifty police offi- j
cers arrived at the track and distrib- j
uted themselves as they had done be- j
fore in the betting ring, among the de
serted booths, in the paddock and in
the grand stand. Attention was given -
particularly to hand booking, the sys
tem by which the gamblers may wager j
with each other. The managers of the
track bad decided to play what they |
thought would be a clever trick. They
failed, however, in their plan. They
sought to induce the bona-fide book
makers of the track to conduct busi
ness and than submit to the arrest
which Chief Lees would command.
Carriages were sent to the racecourse !
to be held in readiness to convey the
bookmakers to the City Prison. Attor- j
ney Ach informed Chief Lees that legi
timate bookmakers would open their
books and if arrested they demanded !
the right to be taken to the City Prison
in carriages. Chief Lees replied that
the Police Department is supplied with
patrol wagons and that every man ar
rested would be taken to the prison in
a patrol wagon. The Chief added that
the city is not in need of conveyances
from the racing gamblers.
This announcement had a depressing
effect. The betting booths remained
deserted. The boards upon which racing
odds are posted remained untouched.
The bookmakers did not relish a ride
through the city in a pelting rain and
they refused to begin business. Ach
and his associates were in a dilemma.
They could not permit the day to pass
without at least a formal violation of the
law. The races were progressing and
hand books were being made wherever
the police were not looking. The bet
ting ring was practically deserted and
in the grand stand the hollow echoes
of the band told the story of empty
benches. The gamblers were forced to
do something and they hired twelve
men to go into the booths and post odds
on the races.
As soon as these men appeared on the
blocks they were placed under arrest
and taken without delay to the Central
police station. The gamblers of Ingle
side had done nothing more than ex
press their contempt for the laws of
the city. The same process will be re
peated to-day and Chief Lees says he
will exercise the same vigilance as be
fore. He says that gambling at the
racetrack must stop as long as the or
dinance of prohibition is a law.
The men that were arrested yester
day were a3 follows:
Frank Hanna, W. Norvell, Leroy Ep
plnger, M. Rosenthal, E. Myers, John
Shanley, Frank* Laceste, R. Nathan,
Walter O'Donneil. Frank Hilton, F.
Howard and J. Deposter. All gave their
occupation as clerks except O'Donneil,
who gave his as a poolbuyer. They
were all released soon after being
booked, each on $500 bonds.
Judge Mogan's courtroom was crowd
ed yesterday morning with habitues of
the racetrack in the expectation that
the cases of the twenty defendants ar
rested Tuesday at the track for violat
ing the new ordinance would come up
for hearing. They were disappointed.
Immediately on the opening of the
court Attorney Ach for the defendants
renewed his motion to dismiss on the
ground that no complaint had been
filed, but the Judge drew his attention
to the fact that he was busy signing
the complaints and declined to grant
the motion.
Prosecuting Attorney Joachimsen,
beside whom Chief Lees sat, said he
was ready to proceed as soon as the
complaints were all sworn to by the
arresting officers, but Ach asked for a
continuance till to-morrow. He' said
he wanted time to examine the com
plaints, as the ordinance under which
the defendants had been arrested was
new, and he might have occasion to
enter a demurrer. The Judge remark
ed that as the prosecution had pro
cured a continuance Wednesday it was
only fair that the defendants should be
granted a continuance, and he would
THE SA^ FBANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, MARCH IT, 1899,
grant the request of the defendants'
attorney. He intimated, however, that
if a demurrer was entered it would
have to be argued and the cases heard
to-morrow, as he would grant no
further continuances.
The four men arrested Wednesday
appeared in Judge Conlan's court yes
terday morning, and by consent the
cases were continued till to-morrow.
That the splendid victory over the
evil of Ingleside has made its impres
sion deeply upon all classes of people,
the following curious communication
to the Board of Supervisors will illus
trate:
San Francisco, March 14. 1899.
To the Honorable Board of Supervisors,
San Francisco: Reading in this morn
ing's Call that the honorable board has
been crowned with success by Mayor
Phelan signing the bill to close the race
track and pool rooms, I would like to
call attention of the honorable board to
places which are just as bad, if not worse,
such as the Cafe Royal, Palace saloon
and Thalia, where these robbing poker
games are running in full blast; percent
age games, where they take from $3 to J4
an hour out of the games, and places
where crooks, thieves, gamblers and cut
throats make it their hangout, and I
think it would, knowing myself you gen
tlemen don't visit these places and do not
know what is going on there. Hoping
you as honorable board members will look
Into these places and do the same with
them as with the pool rooms, I remain
wishing and hoping to see damnable
places closed for ever. Yours respect
ttMy, A VISITOR.
GLOOMY DAY AT INGLESIDE.
A Very Small Crowd Was in
Attendance.
The sport at Ingleside yesterday was
very tame. There was only a handful of
people in the betting ring, and as half of
these" were uniformed officers the out
look for any of the smaller fry to put
a bet down was not promising. The
handicap was declared off and the re
maining four events were decided in rag
time, the "faithfuls" arriving in town
long before dinner time.
The track was muddy and the web
footed equlnes had things all their own
way. The winners were: Annowan, Kitty
Kelly, Granger and Rey Hooker.
If booking had been in progress Major
Hooker would have ruled favorite for the
opening mile run, but he failed to get a
mark. The brown colt, Annowan. took
the lead on the back stretch and, driving
hard at the end, led Ulm over the line
half a length. Bonnie lone was an indif-
C W. L. Stanfield's shifty filly. Kitty Kel
\V. I_. Stanfield's shifty filly. Kitty Kel
ly, was selected to capture the scramble
for two-year-olds and succeeded by a
narrow margin. Little Reiff found it dif
ficult to keep her from bearing in toward
the rail, but she showed exceptional
gameness and just managed to beat Mort
gage out a nose. Garbo, a son of imp.
Foul Shot, secured the show.
The finish to the hurdle handicap was
a very pretty one. Carrying next to top
weight Granger, ridden by Mattler,
showed first into the stretch and in a
ding-dong finish with the light-weighted
Durwood, won by a head.
A mile selling affair concluded the after
i noon's exercises. Ezell's Espionage looked
! the most eligible, but a poor start con
' tributed to her defeat, third being the
i closest up she could finish. Rey Hooker
; trailed Jerry Hunt all the way and then
I beat the Schreiber entry out a head on
I the wire.
Track Notes.
An exercise boy named Rakeman had a
leg fractured by being thrown from a
horse which he was galloping at the
track yesterday morning.
Bookmaker Johnny Fay will leave for
the East early In the. coming week.
"Doc" Tubervllle was indefinitely sus
pended by the judges after the hurdle
event was decided. They viewed with sus
picion his fall from Three Forks. The
old horse does not fence as he once did,
and as there was no booking it hardly
seems plausible that a rider would care
about indulging in a mud bath.
Saturday will not be "Derby day."
Owii-s to emergencies arising over which
It had no control the Ingleside Association
announced the postponement of the run
ning of the California Derby yesterday
until ?

SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Wheat and Parley still lower.
Silver a fraction higher.
Oats fine, corn and Rye dull.
Hay steady and unchanged.
Feedstuffs as before.
Beans easy, but unchanged.
Potatoes continue to advance.
Onions rule weak.
Light receipts of Vegetables.
Butter weak. Eggs higher.
Poultry flrm and Game dull.
Fresh arrivals of Limes.
Fine Oranges firm.
Dried Fruits unchanged.
Provisions as before.
Meats unchanged.
Tlnplate higher.
Linseed Oil advanced.

OUR NATIONAL EXPORTS.
The following figures taken from the bul
letin issued by the Bureau of Statistics of the
Treasury Department show the value of the
principal articles of merchandise exported from
the United States for the eight months ended
February 28. 1899: Breadstuff s, $189,169,034;
cotton, $171,332,251; mineral oils. $33,065,741;
cattle and hogs, $17.935, provisions, $107,
--671.630; total, $521,174,454. The values of the
articles mentioned exported during the eight
months were $540,766,460. The value of the ex
ports of breadstuffs from San Francisco during
the eight months was 56,6*37,717. against $17,
--982,717 for the same time last year.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The weather is cloudy south of the Columbia
River and west of the Rocky Mountains. Rain
has fallen over the entire State of California
and enow in Nevada. With the exception of
the counties bordering on the bay and in
{Southern California the rain since 7 a. m. to
day has been light. The rain will continue at
intervals to-night and Friday.
A portion of the storm has passed eastward
through Central California during the day, and
Is now central ln Nevada.
Conditions are favorable for showery weather
ln California Friday.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight, March 17.
Northern CaliforniaâShowers Friday; fresh
southwest wind.
Southern CaliforniaâShowers Friday; fresh
southwest wind.
NevadaâSnow or rain Friday.
UtahâSnow Friday: cooler.
ArizonaâRain or snow Friday: cooler.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Friday;
fresh southwest wind. G. H. WILLSON,
Local Forecast Official.
EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, March 16.â The sensational rise
In Sugar was largely responsible for the elimi
nation of much of the short Interest in the
rest of the list, material gains appearing
notably among the specialties, while railways
at the high point ranged 31, up, but there were
recessions from the best and the market closed
under pressure. It was a wild day on the Ex
change. Sugar figured for over a quarter of a
million shares, with the aggregate trading
reaching nearly a million shares. The market
was moving cautiously upward when Sugar
burst into prominence and touched 170, a rise
of 29% points over last night, 11 points above
the highest boom prices of 1897. The stock
closed at 159%. The fluctuations were erratic,
an Instance in point being the drop of 10 points
on two transactions from the high point. A
trader sold short 3'"o of the SOO shares traded
In at 170, and within a moment covered at 160,
the only intermediate transaction being 100
shares at 169. Thus the broker realized $3000
on his short sale. There were various rumors
connected with the movement ln Sugar, among
them the report that the trade war had been
settled, that an agreement had been reached
with the Glucose Refining Company and the
Doescher and Arbuckle interests. People ln
the trade generally discredited the reports, pro
fessing Ignorance of any deal upon which they
would likely be posted were It ln existence.
Local Interests Identified with the concerns
mentioned likewise discredited the reports, ex
cept in the case of the president of the sugar
refining company, who made no statement be
fore the market closed. In some quarters the
movement was attributed to a corner ln the
stock and a punishment of the shorts, while
on the other hand a house which was active
in the dealings claimed to be Ignorant of the
reason for the advance, but thought lt of too
great magnitude to have been merely a ma
neuver against the shorts. Tobacco, after
yielding 7% points, made an extreme rise of
10% to 216, in sympathy with the movement in
Sugar. Glucose also rose 9 points to 75; New
York Air Brake touched 200, a rise of 15 points;
Metropolitan Jumped 6% to 255%. Third Avenue
rose 7, Manhatttan 8, Brooklyn Transit 23%,
Consolidated Gas 6% and Burlington 4%. The
other grangers sympathized in a lesser degree
In the advance. Four per cent was the loaning
rate for call money the greater part of the
day, although some loans were made at 3%05
Ser cent. The stiffening of the money rate
ampened the ardor of the speculators and
caused the recession in the latter trading. The
buoyancy of the general market caused a re
sumption of commission house purchases, which
was largely responsible for the rise in the clos
ing hours. Bonds shared to some extent in the
upward movement, but there were some weak
spots which gave the trading an Irregular as
pect. Total sales, $3,325,000.
â¢ United States 3's advanced %. while the new
4's registered reacted % and do coupon ' 4 in
the bid price.

NEW YORK GRAIN AND PRODUCE.
NEW YORK. March 16.â Favorable crop
news from California and the Southwest start
ed a landslide In wheat prices to-day. They
broke 2 cents a bushel in the highest point
of the session and closed 1%Â®1%C under
the previous night. Heavy liquidation, par
ticularly from San Francisco and St. Louis
sources, accompanied the decline, and short
sales as well were on a large scale. The May
option broke to 72% c, closing at 72 7 *,0, with
subsequent trades on the curb at 72%@72%c.
Foreign news was all very weak indicating
declines of %o%d ln Liverpool and 5Â®25 points
at Paris, influenced partly by reported heavy
Argentine and Russian offerings.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET. * .
CHICAGO, March 16.â As the early batch of
crop reports received by commission houses did
not indicate very serious damage to winter
wheat and California confirmed the breaking
of the drought ln that State wheat speculators
commenced the day in a bearish mood. There
were other matters in the early news that like
wise contributed to the selling spirit with
which traders were imbued at the beginning

very slack and Reerbohm's reported Russian
offerings of wheat increasing, while Antwerp

freely In that market.. The bears took fresh
courage when those announcements were made
and scalpers who had bought at the opening
also turned sellers. Between selling out-of
town wheat and selling short the market got
down below the starting prices. Long wheat
came out ln big chunks when May got under
69c, which it did about an hour from the close.
It broke ln the last hour to 674 c and closed at
67%Â®67%c.

CALIFORNIA PRUNES IN DEMAND.
NEW YORK. March 16.â Journal of
Commerce says: '"As prunes are the cheapest
article, relatively, in the line of, dried fruits,
the demand for them of late has been quite
active. Since the large purchases a week or
two ago, which resulted in close concentration
of the spot supplies in the hands of jobbers,
there have been no important sales." Occasional
car lots have been taken but for the most part
sales have been of small quantities, needed
for immediate consumption. The stocks in
flrst hands here are small and In sympathy
with advices from the coast the feeling has
been very firm and the tendency of prices has
been upward. In view of the small offerings
and higher prices demanded for Oregon, more
attention has been, given to the larger sizes
of California. These, however, are in very
small stock In all positions and holders decline
to make any concessions, though by doing so.
It is intimated, business might be materially
Increased. As it is 50-60's California prunes
have within the past few days received a good
deal of attention and have sold well at full
prices, though sales as a rule were of small
lots."
NEW YORK CLEARING HOUSE BUSINESS.
: NEW YORK, March 16.â T0-day's money
transfer between banks at the clearing house,
In payment of debit balances for the day,
was $17,413,862. which Is the largest transfer
of the kind on the records of the Institution.
On January 17, when total check exchanges
were the largest ever recorded, the balance
payments were only $14,404.251, and the high
record until to-day was $17,163,312, on the 4th
of last January. i
Total exchanges to-day -were $238,965,668, j
against $305,341,521 on January 17. The excep- i
tional volume of payments was due to yester
day's payment of the Chicago and Alton pur
chase.

LONDON WOOL SALES.
PON. March 16.â The offerings at the !
wool auction sales to-day numbered 12,659 bales I
and were largely Australian. There were ac
tive competitions for greasy merinos from nil |
directions, and a few suitable parcels were
taken by the American and Russian buyers.
There was strong competition for scour-Is by-
German and Yorkshire buyers. Fine crossbreds
were purchased â  by the home trade at high
rates. A lot of medium scoured Cape of Good
Hope and Natal wool was well represented
and sold briskly at 54 to 10 per cent above the
January sales. The total number of bales sold
to date aggregate 94.500 bales and there re
main to be sold 74,000. The American buyers
have purchased only a few hundred bales so
far, but they are carefully watching the series
for anything ottered suitable for their wants.

THE STOCK MARKET.
Local securities were moderately active, with
an advance in Hana Plantation to $17 25 and
lower prices for Contra Costa. Water.
Mining stocks were rather higher, though
there was not much business.
The Watt Blue Gravel Mining Company of
Nevada County has levied an assessment of 3
cents per share, delinquent May 1.
The Belcher assessment of 10 cents per share
will be delinquent .In the boards to-day.
The New York and Honduras-Rosario Mining
Company dividend of 10 cents per share has
been declared, payable March 20.
The Welsbach Light Company declared a
dividend of 50 cents per share, payable
March 24.

NOTE-In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of th,
day In the order of occurrence as to time Th*
second time column gives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third tide
and th- last or right hand column gives the
fast tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
iivln are additions to the soundings on tha
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (-> precedes the height.
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of the lower low waters.

cf events both for day and evening have
been arranged mure in keeping with the
occasion than the old displays.
There has been a radical change in the |
mode of honoring St. Patrick In recent
years, and now Instead of a drain on
every pocket to pay for bands and regalia
and prancing steeds and the like, the
money is devoted to the welfare of the
wards of the Irish people. It is a change
that is welcomed by every loyal Irish
man. Notwithstanding the more demon
strative feature has 'been abandoned, the
love of the pons and daughters of Erin
for their motherland has not waned. On
the contrary, there is every evidence |
Ifjat with each succeeding year it has
strengthened. And the State and the na
tion Is benerlted thereby, for this venera
tion for the land of their birth makes
Irishmen better citizen* of th*. land of
their adoption.
As an instance of the Increasing re
gard for Ireland and all things Irish may
be cited the spread of the Gaelic . lan-;
; guage in this country, Schools and
classes for the study of this ancient
tongue are multiplying rapidly, .and It is
noteworthy that there are no more ar
dent students than the native sons, who
It would naturally be supposed would
.care comparatively little for the lan
guage of their ancestors, when It Is re
"membered that only a small percentage
- of .those born and reared in Ireland can
speak Gaelic, much leu read or write It.
Now. however, not only those who have
.-left the Green Isle for these shores, but
â¢". native-born Americans of Irish ancestry
as well, are not only mastering Gaelic |
I pronunciation, but are learning to read |
| and write it as well.
: Herewith The Call reproduces a trans- '
i lation of "The Star Spangled Banner" in i
â  Gaelic script and a poem by Mr. William
; J- Egan of San Francisco entitled "The >
Fairy's Visit." Rev. Douglas Hyde. D. !
; D., a recognized authority on Irish liter- :
j ature, wrote to the author praising the i
j sweetness and beauty of his work, which |
| takes rank with the classics.
The preliminary event of the St. Pat
! rick's Day celebration took place last
, evening when the Sons of St. Patrick sat
! down to banquet. It was a notable gath-
I ering, many of the most prominent men
in the State being present.
To-day's ceremonies will for many begin
1 with the celebration of solemn high mass |
at St. Patrick's Church, on Mission street
I between Third and Fourth, at 9 o'clock
! this morning. Rev. Father Horan will be j
tho celebrant, with Father Lane as dea- ,
1 con. Father Keane sub-deacon and Father
i Brennon master of ceremonies. After
mass Father Lane will deliver a sermon j
j on the life and teachings of Ireland's
â¢ saint. Although the feast of St. Patrick .
i is no longer compulsory in the United j
States, every loyal Catholic who possibly
i cat, feels lt his duty to attend mass on i
this day. and in consequence there will be '
! an Immense gathering within the wails
S of old St. Patrick's.
Out at St. Dominic's Church, on Bush
J and Steiner streets, the feast of St. Pat
rick will also be celebrated at 9 o'clock '
i this morning. There will be high mass
i and a panegyric of the saint by Father
i Leo Heagan, O. P., of New York. An im-
I mense attendance at this service ls also :
! assured.
In the evening devotions will be held in
' St. Patrick's Church at 7:30 o'clock. Rev. '
Father Heslan of St. Francis Church will â 
preach the sermon.
The joint convention of Irish societies â 
having in charge the celebration has pre
, pared ample amusement for the day and
evening. In the afternoon the beautiful
I Irish opera, "The Lily of Kiliarney." will
I be given at the Tivoli Opera-house, to
gether with appropriate exercises, in
cluding an oration by Frank T. Shea and
the reading of a poem by Eustace Cul'i- \
â  ran. The full Tivoli company has been
I cast for the- production and as it has !
I been under rehearsal for this occasion for
some weeks the success of the perform- j
1 ance is assured. The complete pro- â -

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, HARCH 17, â  1899.
Division No. 2. Ancient Order of Hi
bernians, will celebrate the day with a

IRISHMEN AT BANQUET.
Knights of St. Patrick Make Merry
Around the Festive .
Board.
.. The twenty-fourth annual banquet of
I Knights of St. Patrick was held last
I evening in the maple room of the Pal
ace. It was the universal comment of
' those present that the occasion was the
! most brilliant and enjoyable in the his
! Tory of the society. Covers were laid for
j over one hundred guests, and the menu
i from the Blue Points to the coffee and
Perfecto Regalias contained every dell-
I cacy imaginable. The banquet hall was
j appropriately decorated. "Old Glory" and
| the ancient flag of Erin gracefully droop
-1 ed their folds together from the head of
| the board. The tables were arranged in
1 three parallel lines extending the entire
I length of the banquet hall. The orches
i tra was embowered from sight, and dur
; ing the banquet rendered all the popular
airs., together with the national songs of
: the United States and Ireland. When it
I played "My Country, 'Tis of Thee" the
i assemblage arose, and at the conclusion
1 of the national anthem cheered it to the
'â  echo. , . -
Prior to the guests taking their seats
' at the banquet grace was asked by. the
Rev. Edward Dempsey. John J. Barrett,
president of the society, in eloquent and
feeling terms delivered the address of
welcome. He vividly pointed to the Irish
man's love for his native land, his loyalty
: to his traditions and his faith to his
i principles. _ *
I Professor David Starr Jordan was the
! first speaker. He responded to "The
i United States." Jordan rather wandered
from his subject, and availed himself of
I the privilege of expressing his personal
THE STAR SPANGLED BANNER.
(3(1 CrtACAd 09Alp6AlCA4)
:'&Z&*XÂ£iG-*ZG'ziCÂ£ZGX$?^i&ZG*&?&
.pinion of the proposed alliance between
this country and England. Professor |
Jordan's remarks were enthusiastically
received.
Richard O'Connor responded to the i
toast "The Day We Celebrate." He elo
quently and appropriately portrayed the
ancient faiths and traditions of Ireland. I
in conclusion he said: ..'â¢',>...
"To-night we, the exiled children of j
Erin, gathered here by the Gold
en Gate, turn to our motherland with '
_. love that neither time nor distance has
lessened, and saluting her from afar we
pray that that liberty to which she has
been so devoted, whose light she has fol- ;
lowed for centuries through tears and ;
through blood, may soon light up her fair
face with a glory that shall never more !
be dimmed."
Robert Lloyd next rendered a vocal
solo that was loudly cheered and greatly
enjoyed. At this stage of the banquet
mirth, music, color, light and good fellow
ship were at their height. It was typical
Irish hospitality, and right royally was
it extended.
Major General Shafter was the next j
speaker. He was accorded a magnificent
reception, and the "Red, White and Blue" ,
was sung in his honor, concluding with
three cheers and a tiger. General Shafter
responded to the "Army," and chiefly â 
showed that the success of the American
arms had been largely due to the bravery
and fidelity of the Irishman as a soldier.
His remarks were frequently interrupted
by volleys of cheers.
Captain Terry, commanding the lowa,
was the next speaker. He responded to ;
the "Navy," and his remarks were con- !
cisc and appropriate, and were frequently ]
punctuated with prolonged cheers. Cap
tain Terry said he had served in the old :
and new "navy, and he drew appropriate I
comparisons between the navy of thirty
years ago and that of to-day.
Following Captain Terry Mayor Phelan ;
responded to the toast of "San Fran-
Cisco " "Our Sister Societies." the Cale- '.
donian Society and the St. Andrew's So
cletv, were responded to respectively by
F. F. Finlav and James b. Webster.
Among the guests were:

Captain S. W. Terry, Mayor Phelan, Professor
David Starr Jordan, James R. Kelly, John J.
Barrett. John Muihern, J. S. Webster, Colonel
T F Barry. F. K. Lane, J. J. Dwyer, F. F.
Finlav, J. Deaay, I* F. Bylngton, T. J. Stan

General Eagan Is Here.
General Charles P. Eagan. ex-commis
sary general, arrived on a late train last
night and registered at the Palace. He,
Ik accompanied by his wife. The gen
eral refused to see any one, as he was
worn out from the journey and retired
immediately.
