HIS DEATH IS
SHROUDED IN
DEEP MYSTERY
James C. Kingsley's
Body Arrives.
WAS IT SUICIDE OR MURDER?
RELATIVES DEMAND . SECRECY
OF THEIR AGENTS.
Son of a Wealthy Eastern Family
Meets a Tragic Death While b
Residing in Central
America.
In the hold of the steamship Acapulco,
which arrived from Mexico yesterday,
a hermetically sealed casket containing
the remains of James Cooke Klngsley,
who was either killed or committed sui
cide in San Salvador, and whose relatives
are trying to conceal the facts that led
| up to his untimely death.
Acting under the Instructions of Ev
arts, Choate & Beaman, the well-known
New York law firm, Wilson & Wilson of
this city made the necessary arrange
ments with the quarantine officers to have
the body landed on Its arrival, but
owing to Its being surrounded by freight
it will not be removed from the ship until
to-day, when it will be forwarded East
in care of J. T. Leavell. a San Salvador
undertaker who was specially engaged by
the deceased's family to look out for its
safe passage. â  ....
The members of the local law firm
pleaded ignorance when asked for the de
tails of Mr. Kingsley's tragic deatn.
Mountford Wilson stated that his firm
was informed of the coming of the body
and simply made arrangements for its
landing in the United States and having
it immediately forwarded to the East.
He could not understand why the news
papers should interest themselves In the
matter, as there was nothing sensational
In Kingsley's untimely taking off.
Undertaker Leavell was also loth to
say anything about the dead man. Ha
gave as his reason for being uncommuni
cative that he had been employed by the
dead man's relatives and had been sworn
to secrecy. He admitted that Mr. Kings
ley was shot, but refused to disclose
where the bullet took effect.
Mr. Kingsley had been engaged in min
ing in Honduras for a number of years
and had amassed a small fortune. He
was said to be the son of the builder of
the Brooklyn bridge, and was known to
have had wealthy relatives ln New York.
Rumor has It that he became mixed up
in a revolution in San Salvador and met
the fate usually accorded a member of a
defeated party. It is also said that the
deceased became tired of continued ill
health and ended his sufferings. Mr.
Kineslev was 40 years of age at the time
of his demise.
A special agent was sent to Honduras
to settle up his estate, and is expected
home on the next steamer.
THE ANCIENT SPIRIT
OF THE IRISH PEOPLE
A RACE RULED $V SPIRITUAL
INSTINCTS.
Rev. Father J. B. McNally Delivers
an Interesting and Instructive .
Lecture in St. Francis'
Church.
"The Spirit of the Irish People" was '
the subject of an interesting lecture at
St. Francis Church on Vallejo street last
evening by Rev. J. B. McNally of West
Oakland. After recounting the noble la
bors of St. Patrick in planting the Chris
tian faith in the hearts of the Irish race
Father McNally continued in part as fol
lows:
The Irish race at home and abroad is ever
ruled by its spiritual instincts rather than by
worldly influences. We find that before Patrick
landed on her shores they were a people host
visions fed their hopes, and while they clung
scrupulously to idolatry, they could be reasoned
with. They were confiding because of their
innate hospitality and generous proclivities, and
they were stubborn because of their consist
ency with conscience. The Irish are pro
nounced by the world to-day to be a wonderful,
a phenomenal people. See Ireland steadily
refusing to drift with the current of opinion
which changed as circumstances of modern
thought moved and directed. They felt that
they were the most ancient of nations and to
them came the others for light and judgment.
Hence they would not be dictated to by the
countries of Europe.
The Irish heart is fashioned to love, and as
love Is the bright attribute of humanity, so the
race clung to the objects of its love, and no
power under heaven could change It. So we
behold them clinging with unswerving fidelity
to its altars, firesides. God and country'- Fam
ine, fire and sword were brought to hear on
that people until only half a million remained
out of that eight million, but no record of a
race recreant to the principles of its faith is
theirs. There never was a change of heart
for an Irishman, even when the darkest storm
clouds gloomed the Irish sky. What the Star
nt Bethlehem was to the Magi, the Holy Sepul
cher was to the weary crusader, the faith of St.
Patrick to the dying Irish soul, so was the
vision of the future sunburst over Ireland free
to the whole Irish race as It lies prostrate un
der the heel of persecution.
Look at that little green bit of an Island, the
one European nation over which no Caesar
ever swayed a scepter. The Dane invaded her
shores and was swept away: the Norman
landed and soon lost his Identity among the
people he sought to conquer. And I say as
long as God allows an Irishman to live just so
lone will that Irishman rise to strike for his
country's freedom. I am convinced that gazing
across the chasm of blood and strife and look
ing down into the depths of the seven-century
gulf of carnage, oppression and murder by Eng
land on the Irish, seeing every horrid specta
cle of blocd and feeling every pang of the tor
tures. I believe that the present race in the
Ireland at home and over the world would
drink the same chalice of suffering to the dregs
over again rather than yield one Jot of her
right or retire one step from the contest or lag
one moment in her march of progress for home
rule; till there Is provided for the genius of the
Celtic race a home and a throne In the bosom
Of a disenthraled and a gloriously regenerated
nation.

ALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.
REFUSES TO SHY
THAT SHE IS
BRANDE` WIFE
Answer That Would
Be Degrading.
ETTA BRANDES AS A WITNESS
SHE DENIES THAT ACCUSED
BEAT LILLIAN.
Prosecution Trying to Show That
Milton, Her Son, Was Prompted
in the Line of Testimony
He Gave.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, March 16.
Mrs. Etta Brandes was the star wit
ness to-day at the murder trial of Wm.
A- Brandes, who is alleged to be her hus
band, but she refused to say what rela
tion she bears to the accused, if any, be
cause the answer would tend to degrade
her. She, too, is charged with the mur
der of Lillian Brandes.
When District Attorney Allen asked:
"Are you any relation to the defendant?"
Mrs. Brandes hesitated and looked at her
attorneys, one of whom, J. E. Bennett,
motioned with his head for her not to an
swer the question. .Mrs. Brandes replied
then that she declined to answer. Judge
Hall advised the Woman that she would
not be obliged to answer if the answer
would tend to degrade her. There was
another pause and Judge Hall then asked
witness if such was the ground on which
she declined to respond, and she assented
with a half-suppressed "Yes, sir."
At the preliminary hearing the fact had
developed that Brandes was at one time
the husband of Mrs. Mary Arrelanes of
San Francisco and that ho had never
been divorced from her.
Before Mrs. Brandes took the stand
this forenoon lit; Milton Brandes, son
of the accused, was recalled and among
the questions asked by Attorney Sawyer
was whether Liilian had ever said "what
she would rather do than go back to the
ranch." The boy replied:
"She said she would sooner kill herself,
or m*-, or my mother, than go back to
the country."
The child was then cross-examined,
when it developed that witness had spent
considerable time last evening with his
father's attorneys and that they had
asked him the questions answered by him
In court this morning. Attorney Bennett
vigorously objected to all this. "Do they
intend to impeach us?" he asked. Judge
Hall, however, ruled that it was proper
for 'the prosecution to try and show that
Milton had been prompted and induced to
give a certain line of testimony, though
the line of examination may be directed
to reflect even upon Attorneys Bennett
and Sawyer. * * i
The cross-examination that followed
mixed the young witness on certain
points, adding color to the prosecution's
Intimation. Before he left the stand he
said positively that Brandes never scolded
Lillian or swore at her, nor nad Lillian
had any scratches on her face.
Mrs. Brandes was next called. Her thin,
pale face was veiled, but at the request
of a Juror the veil waa removed. Almost
at the outset she contradicted her son's
testimony, declaring that Brandes did
scold Lillian on Tuesday night, Novem
ber 15 last, though she Insisted that Lil
lian and her father always manifested
affection for each other. On the Sunday
previous to that Tuesday witness and
Lillian, while out riding, had had a dis
pute. As a result Brandes had concluded
to send the girl back to thÂ« country. On
Monday afternoon witness had missed $15
which had been saved for rent and which
was partly to be used to pay Lillian's
fare to the ranch.
No search was made Monday night, but
en Tuesday morning between 1 and 2
o'clock Brandes brought the girl into wit
ness' room, scolded the girl and spanked
the boy Milton. She said the girl was
fully dressed, even to her shoes and stock
ings. On Tuesday evening, after dinner,
the money search was begun and on Fri
day the money was found on the dining
room table.
"On Saturday morning Brandes came
home at 5:30," continued Mrs. Brandes.
"He went to bed and was asleep when I
awoke from noises which I thought were
downstairs. I went to Lillian's room and
found her hanging to her bedpost by
some aprons."
Witness then went on to affirm that she
called Brandes and they went down to
gether. He took the girl down and tried
to do the best he could for her. Attorney
Sawyer then asked:
"How did Lillian Brandes come to her
death?" Mr. Melvln's objection to this
question was sustained.
"Did Mr. Brandes hang Lillian before or
after death?" continued Attorney Sawyer.
"No sir," snappishly responded the wit-
ness. , ,
"Did he cause the red marks you saw
about her neck?" asked Sawyer, and wit
ness again replied negatively. She also
testified that Lillian had- no symptom of
\ chill or pains in the head of the
Friday evening prior to her death and
' that at that time the girl had no black
' and blue marks on her hands.
I On cross-examination Mrs. Brandes tes
i tified that one of the conditions of Lil
lian's advent into the Brandes home in
Berkeley was that she should call Mrs-
Brandes by her first name 'Etta and
Brandes "Al," because she did not like
to be called a stepmother. Her reason
( for knowing that Lillian was B , r VÂ£s?
: daughter was the fact that he had intro
i duced her as such to several persons. To
most of the important questions put by
the prosecution witness would reply that
she "didn't remember." However, Mrs.
Brandes was "always good to the girl,
gave her money to buy ribbons with and
took her to" amusements. Mrs. Brandes
admitted that she did not get along well
with Lillian. f _ â , â ,
"Lillian went to school Monday. , con
tinued witness, "but we kept her out
Tuesday and the balance of the week be
cause there was no sense in sending her.
The missing $15. witness said, had been
given to her by Brandes at various times
and ln various amounts, and she first
knew of its absence from the bureau
drawer on Monday when Milton, her real
son, told her. Milton yesterday testified
that he did not tell that Lillian took the
money until half an hour after the search
1 had commenced. f*-~_L â
Mrs. Brandes said she "supposed there
might have been other money kept in
the house elsewhere," and that the rent
usually came due on the 4th or uth of
the month. She denied that she had ever
told Mrs. May Godfrey that "Lillian was
always causing trouble and was a thief.
Before court adjourned till to-morrow
morning, when Mrs. Brandes will be fur
ther cross-examined, the defense called
Florenz Vasquez of Salinas, by whom
they desired to show that the reputations
of two of the State's witnesses, Ramon
Arana and Mrs. Mary Galboa, were bad. .
Vasquez said it was very bad, but on
cross-examination admitted he had not
heard their reputations discussed since
nine years ago.
Bids for Lake Merritt Gates.
OAKLAND, March 16.â Board of
Works received bids this morning for con
structing the necessary culvert, canal and
gates and piling to complete the improve-
ments adopted for the work on Lake Mer
rltt. Several bids were opened and re
ferred to the City Attorney. The figures
were as follows: Cotton Bros., $24,850:
J. A. Marshall. $20,680; Dundon Bridge and
Construction ' Company, $21,863; Williams
Bros., $21,530.
EVANGELIST MOODY
SPEAKS AT OAKLAND
STIRRING ADDRESSES AT THE
UNION REVIVAL.
Big Audiences Gather to Hear the
Words of Wisdom Fall From
His Lips.
OAKLAND, March Every niche of
the First Presbyterian Church was oc
cupied to-night in attestation of the fact
that Dwight L. Moody has lost none of
his popularity or forcefulness as an
evangelist.
The choir and platform were occupied
by ministers of all denominations from
the bay cities, as the revival is a union
movement of all the churches. The evan
gelist has not been in California for ten
years, but he retains his power of con
viction, and is more ready than ever to
draw from his apparently endless fund of
anecdotes. The revival opened this after
noon at the First M. E. Church, and al
though stormy weather prevailed the
large church was packed.
Mr. Moody delivered powerful addresses
both afternoon and evening, and inter
jected so much wit into them that his
audience was laughing every few min
utes. His tact in turning the cause of the
outburst of mirth into a pointed truism is
marvelous and a powerful factor in re
taining his spell over his vast congrega
tions. He made many kindly references
to newspaper men, and apparently he
does not consider they are any more in
need of salvation than the average
being.
Referring to the Philippine question Mr.
Moody said; : - "'- 'â -â 
"My view of the Philippine situation
may be given in a nutshell. We have got
into It and we must go through with it.
The best way to get out of some diffi
culties is to "manfully go on with them
and go through with them. We forced
President McKinley. obviously against
his will, into the war with Spain. Popu
lar sentiment should now stand by him in
carrying forward its results, and it will.
If this nation had known that the civil
war would have brought emancipation as
a military necessity, the nation would
never have entered upon that war. The
abolitionists might have been willing to
do so, but not the body of the people. The
present situation is somewhat similar.
Our nation and people will have to face
the military and other necessities fol
lowing the war with Spain.
"I think the Catholic church is now see
ing the importance of education as well
as righteousness as a means of the eleva
tion of peoples. The Pope has come out
and advised the whole Catholic world to
read the Bible fifteen minutes every day.
This is something new and a very hopeful
sign. * T V.
"On every ground I am very much en
couraged with* the outlook for Christian
work in this country."

THE SAN FRANCISCO, CALL, FRIDAY, MARCH 17, 1899.
NEW COUNCIL
MAY SETTLE
WATER BATES
Recent Rain May Be
a Live Factor.
FAREWELL OF SOLID SIX
CANVASSING RETURNS MUST
BE FIRST BUSINESS.
After Being Declared Elected the
New Council Will Take the
Oath and Proceed to
Do Business.
Oakland Offlce San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, March 16.
It Ib just possible that the solid six may
not have the satisfaction of fixing a
water rate for this city for the next fis
cal year. This Important duty may fall
to the new Council. When that body
meets next Monday night it must take up
first of all the election returns and
canvass them, nor can any other busi
ness be taken up until this tusk be fin
ished, although the Council can adjourn
from time to time as convenience may re
quire.
It will take all of one session to per
form this task, and although the water
ordinance will have been published th-3
necessary length of time, lt is not likely
that It will come up for final passage on
Monday night. After the election returns
are canvassed the Councilmen-elect can
at once take the oath of oflice before the
City Clerk, and there Is a doubt as to
whether they cannot at once demand
their seats. It is said that the Interpre-
I tation of the charter does not prevent
i them doing so. At the following meet
! ing of the Council on Monday, March 27,
j the old Council, should lt desire, can take
'up the water ordinance, ibut should the
j least amendment be carried it will have
! to go to print again for five days, and
â¢ this will Indisputably carry it into the
new administration.
Should the solid six hold together they
can do as they please at a minute's no
tice next Monday night or the follow
ing Monday by fixing water rates oy a
resolution, which requires no publishing.
As not one of the six has anything to
' hdpe for politically in the future it would
! not be surprising if some grand coup
i were attempted. The absolute overthrow
i of Messrs. Woodward and Watkinson, the
: only two who sought vindication at the
polls, very clearly expresses public feel
j ing and adds to the assumption that the
' six might attempt almost anything before
its farewell. '-â  '-.- -- â :- :-: -.â  ? 'â '
The prevailing rain storm has added
interest to the water question. It has
rendered the Contra Costa company in
dependent of the Dingee company and
has caused a rumor to be circulated that
ROMANCE GETS
KNOCKED OUT
J{ Fin -de - Sieele .Hero's Glory
Dimmed by a .Monetary
Consideration.
THERE is cause for rejoicing. Marlon Magallon, who some little while
ago posed as an early century hero ln a very fin de sieele divorce, Is
not so noble after all. In no way has he been false to the traditions
of his times and his romantic separation from a supposedly beloved wife
is nothing more nor less than a satisfactory dissolution, for a monetary
consideration, of a marriage that was disagreeable all round.
On September 4, 1898, Virginia Rieffel and Marlon Magallon were married.
Previous to her wedding to Magallon Miss Rieffel had been the betrothed
wife of Frank Pichon. After his engagement to the girl of his choice,
Pichon went to 'the Klondike to. win a fortune, and for several years noth
ing was heard of him. y'-ffff.
After waiting three years Miss Rieffel concluded that Pichon was dead
and accepted the hand of Marion Magallon. On the night of the wedding,
just as the guests were drinking to the health of the newly married couple,
Pichon put in his appearance. Then it was all up with Marion. Mrs. Ma
.gallon decided that she still loved Pichon first best and for all time, and
the husband was sent out with witnesses to- give his wife substantial
ground for divorce. â¢ 'â¢-,_' V
On March 3 Virginia Rieffel-Magallon was granted a divorce from Ma
rion Magallon on the ground of infidelity. On March 8 the young divorcee
and Frank Pichon were made man and wife by a Justice in Virginia City,
Nev.
Now that Virginia is wedded to the man of her choice, the story has
leaked out that Magallon'does not belong to the medieval school of ro
mantic heroes, but that he is a very up to date young man. Five hun
dred dollars was the consideration that induced him to leave his young
bride free to wed the man she loved. ; .
"Virginia lived with Magallon for about five days after the wedding,"
said the father of the young wife. "Then she came home and said she was
going mad and that she could not live without Pichon. Then I went : to
Magallon and we talked business. He demanded $300 to set the girl free and
I consented. A few days after he wanted $350, and so he went on until he
finally reached $500. I paid him over $500 in gold, and also $100 to Abe Ruef,
his attorney. That $100 explains why the defendant lawyer took the plain
tiff's side when his legal representative was too ill to appear."
Mr. and Mrs. Pichon leave this mornrng for Alaska, where the young
husband has several rich claims. Magallon Is about town blowing in the $500
he demanded and received for his wife's freedom.
the consolidation proceedings are not yet
final. When the Council takes up the
water rate question the Contra Costa
people cannot make the argument that
the drought has made it necessary for
them to take on large additions to their
plant, for the report to-day shows that
the company's lake at San Leandro has
risen seven feet and Is still rising. As
the lake covers 411 acres, this enormous
addition to its supply has effectively
stopped all talk of a water famine next

PLACE OF UTTER DARKNESS.
San Bruno Road Residents Anxious
to Have Electric Lights.
There is a section of this city where
neither gas nor electric lights shine. This
is on the San Bruno road and the cross
streets and avenues leading therefrom to
Mission street. A similar condition of af
fairs can be found on the Ingleside road
from Mission street west to the ocean.
The residents of the San Bruno road
complain of this municipal neglect and
Indifference. They say that since the
electric cars have been running out to
the Five-Mile -House travel has increased
sufficiently to justify the Supervisors in
having lights extended as far as the cars
go. Both the San Bruno road and the
Mission street property owners and resi
dents have joined in a request to have
electric lights placed on Silver avenue.
From present indications the San Bruno
road is destined to become a favorite sec
tion of the city for homes for mechanics
and industrious laboring men. But the
people want artificial lights before they
commence to build homes.
The want of electric lights on the In
gleside road is equally felt by the people
residing in the southwest section of the
Mission. _________________
WONG FONG UNDER
ARREST FOR MURDER
SAID TO BE THE VICTIM OF A
PLOT.
Charged With the Crime for Which
Ong Foon Is on Trial at
Santa Rosa.
Lieutenant Price and a Constable from
Santa Rosa arrested a Chinaman named
Wong Forig on suspicion of the murder
of a Chinaman in Santa Rosa several
years ago. It is believed that the ar
rest is a move on the part of the friends
of Ong Foon. who is now being tried for
the same murder, to implicate another
and thereby lessen the chances for a con
viction of Ong Foon.
The English Educational Society, of
which Ong Foon is a member, has been
very active in his defense, and the bitter
ness existing between this organization
and the slave owners, who are supposed
to be behind the prosecution, has caused
the friends of Ong Foon to do everything
in their power to secure his acquittal.
Ong Foon was arrested some months
ago at the instigation of Wong Sam, a
slave owner, who charged him with at
tempting to extort money from one of
the slave girls. Ong Foon was acquitted
on this charge and the Chinamen inter
ested in prosecuting him claimed that the
case had been "fixed." The old charge
of murder which had been hanging over
Ong Foon's head was brought up again,
and as he stepped from the courtroom
after being acquitted of the charge of at
tempted extortion he was arrested for
murder.
It is asserted that the prosecution has
a clear case against Ong Foon. lie lias
been positively identified as the murderer
by both Chinese and white witnesses.
When the case first came up the defense
sought to prove an alibi, but later
changed to self-defense. The prosecution
claims that this Is a sign of weakness and
further assert that money was offered to
square the case. Besides the murder
charge pending against Ong Foon in
Santa Rosa, it is said that he is also un
der Indictment for a murder committed
In Sacramento the same year. A money
settlement was made in this 'case, and
Ong Foon agreed to leave Sacramento
and not return. He went back once and
barely escaped with his life. Several wit
nesses from Sacramento who are familiar
with the latter case have been subpenaed
to testify against Ong Foon in the case on
trial at Santa Rosa.
Wong Fong denies that he was ever in
Santa Rosa and that he knows anything
of the murder of which he is accused.
He bears a good reputation with the
police, and the latter are of the belief that
his arrest is the result of an effort to aid
Ong Foon. ____________________
STORY OF SANTIAGO
TOLD BY SHAFTER
HISTORY OF FORMER CAM
PAIGNS IN CUBA.
Big Audience Greets the General
With Enthusiastic Applause.
Lecture for the Benefit of the
Christian Commission.
General Shafter received a rousing re
ception in the Y. M. C. A. Auditorium
last night, where he delivered a lecture
on "The Campaign at Santiago de Cuba."
The hall was crowded, and when the gen
eral stepped out on the stage he was
greeted with a storm of applause.
The evening's entertainment was
opened with the singing of the "Star
Spangled Banner" by Miss Grace I. Davis.
Secretary H. J. McCoy then introduced
Mayor Phelan as chairman of the even
ing. Mr. Phelan in introducing General
Shafter said:
"General Shafter is a Caiifornian as
well as a soldier. As students of contem
poraneous history, we regard him as a
man of note and as citizens we look upon
him as our neighbor. We are thankfm
that he has come back to us unharmed
from his trying campaign and hope that
be will spend the balance of his days In
our beloved State, for when he was given
his choice of departments did he not se
lect California? This is an honor to our
State and should help endear him to us."
General Shafter then stepped forward,
and after the applause had subsided open
ed his speech by saying a good word for
the Army and Navy Christian Commis
sion, which has done so much for the
soldiers and sailors. He then gave a
brief general history of previous expedi
tions sent from temperate climates into
the tropics.
"The first of these expeditions," he
said, "was in 1742. The English at that
time sent a large force under General
Vernon to capture Santiago. The troops
landed, and although altogether unop
posed, were forced to withdraw after
having marched but a few miles. The
soldiers were utterly prostrated. The
next expedition was In 1761, when the
English landed another army of 24,000.
A number of these were negroes and
some were colonists from New York. Of
this army 17,000 died from the tropical
fevers and at the end of the campaign
only 700 were fit for service, In 1801 Na
poleon sent thirty-six ships of the line
and sixteen frigates to the south of Cuba.
Twenty thousand men were landed, but,
although the campaign only lasted about
three weeks, there were only 2000 men
who managed to get back to France. The
rest had succumbed to the dreaded fevers
of the tropics.
"I give you these figures simply to show
what our men had to contend with. These
were all carefully planned campaigns.
Ours was not. We had not intended land
ing an army in the islands until the fall,
but when the news of the sudden appear
ance of Cervera's fleet in the harbor
reached Washington we were ordered to
move at once. 1 received the dispatch at
night, and the next morning 10,000 men
were moving. It has been said that the
accommodations for the men were not
sufficient. The men were crowded some
what, but the weather was fine and the
troops suffered little or no inconvenience.
Yet had we had more time better arrange
ments would certainly have been made."
He then gave a detailed account of the
campaign, and concluded by saying a
good word for the boys in blue, the fight
ing privates, to whom he gives the whole
credit of the victory.
Among those on the stage who acted as
vice-presidents were:
Colonel W. R. Smedberg, Edward Coleman,
Rev. Dr. J. N. Beard, Judge E. A. Belcher,
E. 1' Vlnir.g, General R. H. "Warfleld, C. A.
Maydwell, W. W. Chase, Judge E. D. Saw
yer Major It. 11. Noble, Captain E. H. Plum
in. r W. H. Mills, Rev. Dr. C. 17. Locke, D.
Edward Collins, Rev. Dr. E. B. Spalding, Rev.
Dr. George C. Adams. Major S. F. Thorn,
Colonel F. M. Coze, Colonel "W. H. Forwood,
Major F. *'. Lord, Captain F. Greene, Captain
T. I". Raymond, Major N. Kenyon, Major G.
F. Downey, General J. B. Marshall, R. H.
Chamberlain, N. H. Jacks. General Babcock,
Captain Charles Goodall. Captain O. Eldridge,
Colonel C. Mason Clone, Major W. (>.
Gould, Bishop Nichols, 11. J. McCoy. Captain
McKlttrick, Rev. Dr. John Hemphill, V.*. S.
Goodfellow, J. S. Bunnell, Judge Morrow,
Major W. B. Hooper, I. J. Truman, C. S.
Wright, E. E. Kelly, M. D., J. D. McKee,
l:. C, Wright, Rev. Dr. James, Robert Bruce,
c. S. Holmes, W. Bradford, N. P. Cole and
C. B. Perkins.
The proceeds of the lecture will be de
voted to the Army and Navy Christian
Commission.
CRIME IS PREVENTED
BY A PLUCKY WOMAN
WILLIAM BRONSON ASSAULTED
ON MISSION STREET.
.
One of His Assailants Is Under Ar
restA Bottle of Knockout
Drops as Evidence.
A bold attempt at robbery was made
on Mission street, between Third and
Fourth, last night, by two men. One of
'them, Frank O'Rourke, is now under ar
arrest. The other managed to get away.
The two men were first noticed in a
Mission-street saloon with William Bron
son. Bronson was very much under the
influence of liquor and was treating
freely. The three men left the saloon to
gether, and a woman who was passing
at the time heard one of them say: "This
fellow has got money; we'll do him." The
words alarmed her and she watched the
trio. They had proceeded but a short dis
tance when one of them struck Bronson
and knocked him down.
The woman immediately ran down to
Third street, where she found Police Offi
cer F. Callahan and told him what was
going on. He hurried to the scene and
found that one of the men was trying to
force the contents of a vial down Bron
son's throat. His companion gave the
alarm when he saw the officer aproach
ing and took to his heels. O'Rourke was
captured and locked up in the City Prison
charged with attempted robbery.
The contents of the vial will be analyzed
to-day. It is supposed to contain knock
out drops.

SAN FRANCISCO, Cal., March 15, 1899^Sealed
proposals, In triplicate, will be received here
until 12 o'clock m., April 14, 1599,. and then
opened, for new boiler, repairs to engine and
docking, cleaning and repairing U. S. steam-
er General McDowell. Government reserves
right to reject or accept any or all bids In
whole or in part. Preference given to articles
of domestic production, conditions of price
and quality (including in the price of foreign
productions the duty thereon) being equal,
and such preference given to articles of Amer-
ican production produced on the Pacific Coast
to extent of consumption required by the pub-
lic service there. Information furnished on
application to undersigned. Envelopes con-
taining proposals should bo indorsed "Pro-
posals for new boiler, docking, etc., of steam-
er," and addressed to J. M. MARSHALL,
Deputy Quartermaster General, Chief Q. M.

MRS. ADAMS TO
FACE ACCUSERS
IRIS MORNING
The Inquest Will Be
Held To-Day.
DETECTIVES ARE CONFIDENT
BAY EVIDENCE AGAINST THE
WOMAN IS STRONG.
Alleged Murderess Breaks Down and
Weeps, but Steadfastly Declares
Her Innocence of Her
Child's Death.
The Coroner's inquest to determine the
cause of the death of 2-year-old John
Richard Gray, who died In frightful
agony from the effects of carbolic acid
last Monday morning, will be held at 10
a. m. to-day. Detective George Graham
and Sergeant Duke will be on hand to
present the evidence gathered by them
tending to show that the pretty, innocent
little baby boy was the victim of an un
natural mother wno sought to rid herself
of an incumbrance and at the same time
revenge herself on the father of the child,
who abandoned her after living with her
for several years.
At first glance the officers appear to
have built up a strong case against the
woman. They will show that her reputa
tion for morality was such as to place her
beyond the pale of decency. They will
show that she was at daggers* points
with Wilson Gray, the father of the child,
and will depend on this man to prove that
she had threatened to make away with
the fruit of their unlawful relations. They
will introduce evidence to show that it
would have been next to Impossible for
the child to have voluntarily swallowed
the quantity of the caustic fluid which
the autopsy disclosed to have been taken
into its stomach. They will try to make
capital . out of the discrepancies in the
statements of the accused woman and her
10-year-old brother, Harrison Tyrrell, in
regard to the finding of the flask contain
ing the poison in the vacant lot in the
rear of the humble home on Parker ave
nue and of its being carelessly thrown
into the back yard where it is alleged to
have been picked up by the baby.
They will also introduce evidence in
support of their contention that Mrs.
Adams took the child into the outhouse
and forced the burning poison down its
throat. This evidence will consist of the
testimony of Chemist Green of the Board
of Health, who will go on the stand and
swear that the boards torn from the floor
in the outhouse by the detectives and
submitted to him for analysis were sat
urated with carbolic acid.
But the last and most important evi
dence against the woman will be the tes
timony of Officer John P. Herlihy, who
will swear that he saw Mrs. Adams come
out of Loehr's drug store, at the corner
of Devisadero and Geary streets, at 5:45
Sunday night,' tear the label from a bot
tle and board a car going in the direc
tion of her home. The officer's testimony
will be corroborated, in so far as it relates
to the purchase of poison by a woman
at tho hour mentioned, by Drug Clerk
George Purlenky. ,
This is the case of the detectives, and
while it appears convincing it has its
weak points. Herlihy and the clerk both
say the woman who purchased the acid
at Loehr's on Sunday night and whose
peculiar actions while standing on the
corner waiting for a car attracted the at
tention of the officer wore a black cape
of some woolly material trimmed with
fur Mrs. Kelly, the mother of Mrs. > A
dam's said yesterday that her daughter
had not owned a cape for at least five
years Moreover the neighbors of 117
Parker avenue say they never saw Mrs.
Adams wear a cape of any description.
No one in the vicinity of the Tyrrell house
saw Mrs. Adams going or returning on
Rimdav night. Her father and grown
brothers say she was in the house all the
evening or at least until they retired,
about 9 o'clock, although she was not in
fhe same room with them and might
Easily have left the house for a short
time without their being aware of the
? a The trip from the Tyrrell house to
TnelM-'s drugstore can be made in seven
m?nutes if there is no delay In waiting for*
a car Allowing seven minutes for there
turn 'trip and five minutes for completing
the ma-chase of the acid Mrs. Adams
could have secured the poison and re
turned home again in twenty minutes.
But Officer -Herlihy says the woman he
saw waited on the corner several min
utes before a car came along, so if Mrs.
Adams is that woman she must have been
away from â  the house fully twenty-five
minutes, which is possible, but if her
father and brother speak the truth, hard
ly probable. . 'fif^jt
The Tyrrell house was again searched
from garret to cellar by the detectives
yesterday for the cape Mrs. Adams must
have worn Sunday night if Officer Herll
hy's identification is to be clinched. They
also raked over the refuse in the yard and
under the house for the three-ounce bot
tle supposed to have contained the poi
son purchased of Drug Clerk Purlenky.
They worked industriously, but found
nothing. f-y .. , .
The only evidence secured by the detec
tives yesterday in addition to the report
of Chemist Green was a statement signed
by Mrs. Sophia Trumpower, Mrs. Adams'
sister, to the effect that the accused wom
an purchased three ounces of carbolic
acid, to be used as a douche by Mrs.
Trumpower, about a year ago. This Is
supposed to prove that Mrs. Adams lied
when she told the officers she had never
used or purchased carbolic acid at any I
time.
The detectives are somewhat disap
pointed over the disappearance of the
scraps of the "poison" label picked out !
of the gutter by Officer Herlihy and |
handed to Purlenky. The latter placed I
them on a shelf behind some bottles In I

Frank Danehardt, the store boy, saw

thinking they were of no value threw !
them into the refuse heap. Danehardt
remembers the circumstance distinctly,
but the little bits of paper are gone be- ,
yond recovery.
While the officers of the law are search- j
ing high and low for evidence to convict i
the woman they firmly believe to b* I
guilty of a heinous crime, the alleged
murderess shows not the slightest fear
of the final outcome of their investiga- |
tion.
Mrs. Adams is certainly a peculiar and
in some respects a remarkable woman.
If she is guilty of the crime of murdering
her little son she is more than a degen
erate; she is a freak, for while she has ;
a weak face it is lacking in the usual
characteristics of an evil mind, and is
not such a face as a physiognomist would j
expect to find possessed by one who could |
deliberately destroy her own offspring. I
But she appears to be dull of comprehen- j
sion, and yesterday, for the first time I
since the tragedy, she appeared to j
awaken to a realization of the serious
ness of her position. She spent the great.!
portion of the day in tears. She wept co
piously and continuously in strange con
trast to her former indifferent demeanor.
But she still professed her confidence in
her early release from custody.
"They cannot keep me here," she said
as she dried her tears with a handker
chief. "They cannot find any evidence to
prove that I poisoned my baby. Why
should I wish to kill him when I loved
him so much. I do not see how that po
liceman, Herlihy, can say he saw me
come out of the drug store Sunday iiigiit.
I was not there. I was not out of the
house Sunday night, not even to the front ,
gate, and besides, I have not owned nor
worn a cape for years. So you see the |
woman he saw could not have been me.
I know where Loehr's drugstore is, as I 7
have often passed the place on the cars
or when out for a walk, but 1 was never
there in my life. That policeman is very
much mistaken or else he is lying." .
Mrs. Adams has a big red mark on her i
neck caused by the acid coming in con- j
tact with her: skin when she caught her <
child up in her arms When she found him
crying at the foot of the steps in the
rear of the house, so she says. All evi
dence obtainable on the subject goes to
show the accused woman dearly loved the
child she is accused of poisoning, and the
only possible motive for the crime is that
advanced by her accuser, Wilson Gray,
the child's father. Gray's testimony, un
less substantiated by other evidence, will
have little weight with a jury. The
woman is bad, and disgusting in her man
ner of life, but it remains to be proved
that she Is any worse than her accuser
and former companion. In. the meantime
she is entitled to the benefit of any doubt
as to her guilt that may exist.
Attorney James Sweeney will represent
Mrs. Adams at the inquest to-day. and
acting under his Instructions she will /not
testify.

The Board of Health held its regular
monthly meeting at the City Hall Wednes
day, during which the health report
for February was submitted. There were
237 marriages, 354 births and 656 deaths.
Ten deaths were due to diphtheria, 16 to
measles. 5 i.to scarlet fever, 22 to typhoid
fever and 8? to whooping cough, making a
total of 61 fatalities from contagious dis
eases. The most of the contagious cases,
of which there were 144 reported, occur
red -in four distinct localitiesâ in the vi
cinity of Twenty-third and Florida
streets, Eighth and Harrison streets, the
neighborhood of Kearny and Union
streets. The fourth locality is that of the
Presidio Reservation. However, the 18
cases at that place extended over several
months. "bib ' â 
Dr. Williamson called attention to the
fact that the death rate was much great
er than that of the births, and stated
that the latter number' was certainly too
low. There was no doubt, he held, that a
large number of births each month had
not been reported. The Health Officer
and Secretary of the Board were request
ed to bring to the attention of all phy
sicians, nurses, etc., the legal obligation
of reporting such events.

scarlet fever and diphtheria were preva
lent in the Latin quarter of the city and
that the people of that locality, through

DAN McCARTY'S "old white hat" once more adorns the cranium of its <
gifted owner. Dan's fuzzy tile of snow white has been tossed about the _,
world everywhere from jail to the battle ground in Manila, and it still <
retains its shape, color and place in the affections of the eccentric Dan. +
In fact. Dan thinks more now of his ancient "roof" than he ever did dur- <
ing the thirty odd years that it has protected the machinery which enables -
him to make sharp deals in horseflesh. â
Ever since last July Dan's "old white hat" has been in Manila. When the <
First Nebraska Regiment left this city Colonel Chiton borrowed Dan's tile ,
for good luck. It is needless to say that the colonel gave the famous hat the <
place of honor in his tent. He went further than that, for on the night of the -
flrst battle with the insurgents the colonel loaned the hat to one of his sol- .
diers named Murray, who wore it during the midnight skirmish. After the <
battle Colonel Colton picked up a Spanish bullet and had it polished and -
made into a pin, which was then attached to the side of the hat. Dan never ex- <
pected to get his headgear back until the Nebraska regiment returned, but for -
some reason or other Colonel Colton sent the ancient mansard back on the .
last steamer. â . <
The soldier to whom the task of returning the hat was entrusted walked -
into Charlie Newman's saloon the other day and, addressing Charlie ir! a <
garrulous manner, said: "Say, old man. Colonel Colton told me to leave Dan " (
McCartv's skvpiecc with you. I'd like to have time to hunt 'White Haf .
up myself to tell him all the fun we had with his 'old white hat.' but l'e ,
got to catch the next train East." 'b y b b
When Newman presented 'White Hat' with his decorated headgear fresh
from the battle ground of Manila bystanders heard Dan murmur something (
about a "small bot," but they neglected to mention whether or not they
heard the proverbial pop which usually follows the mention of the efferves- _
cent liquid. , Â,-.'
When the California boys come back they will all speak reverently of Dan .
McCarty and his "old white hat." "
It was the only object that they laid eyes upon in far away Manila which
reminded them sharply of "dear old Frisco." ,__.â-
And so it is that the memory of Dan McCarty's "old white hat has -
become immortalized. i, '

THE LAW WILL BE OBSERVED.
Ingleside Coursing Club Will Run on
Sunday, but No Pools Will
Be Sold.
A surprisingly large gathering of leash
men and patrons of coursing was
present last evening at Pythian Castle to
hear the ultimatum of the Ingleside
Coursing Club, which was to the effect
that a meetingâ probably the lastâ would
be held at the Ingleside Park on Sunday.
Furthermore, that in accordance with an
ordinance recently passed by the Super
visors in reference to horse racing and
coursing, no pool selling of any descrip
tion will be permitted by the club on the
grounds during the meeting.
Mr. Shannon, the president of the club,
stated that the organization did not pro
pose to violate the law in any shape or
form, but owing to the face that a num
ber of dog owners had trained their
charges with the object of running them
on Sunday, the directors decided to hold
one more meeting in. the old park; but
that In future coursing cannot take place,
as the club cannot afford to offer the dog
owners sufficient financial Inducements to
meet the expenses incurred by the dog
men in training and keeping hounds in
condition for the chase.

PATROLMAN
BEACKMAN
IS INDICTED
Grand Jury Takes
Summary Action.
A BENCH WARRANT ISSUED
â¢ yy'--
INVESTIGATING PURCHASES OF
CITY SUPPLIES.
Excessive Prices Are Being .Paid, It
Is Alleged, for Material Used
on Public Buildings and
Apparatus.
The Grand Jury indicted Patrolman
Cassius M. Blackmail at its meeting yes
terday afternoon. The inquisitorial body
has taken action in a number of minor
cases, but up to the present time has ndt
attacked large game, although the mem
bers are not slow to say there is much
in the affairs of the city that needs prob
ing. Blackman, who was a policeman on
duty in the Mission, is accused of the
robbing of a miner named Cornelius
Cronin from Nevada.
Testimony In the case was taken last
week, when a number of witnesses were
heard. The Police Department Is anxious
to apprehend Blackman and have him
punished, as his conduct was a reflection
on the reputable men of the force. The
indictment was placed on the secret file
in Judge Dalngerfield's court and a bench
warrant issued immediately. .^- .
H. M. Black, acting chairman of the
Committee on Mayor, City Hall Commis
sioners and Board of Supervisors, has In
stituted an inquiry into the supplying of
material to the various departments of
the city. It has been found that the mu
nicipality is paying about one-third more
for its material than would a private in
dividual. Again certain favored firms are
permitted to supply the city with articles
which they do not ordinarily handle. Thus
a hardware firm supplies plumbing ma
terials which it does not keep in stock
but which it must, go outside and pur
chase and for which it charges an extra
price. . ,
An effort will also be made by the com
mittee to fix the responsibility for the
present condition of the City Hall. The
City Hall Commissioners have turned the
big building over to the Supervisors, the
funds in their charge being exhausted.
The Supervisors decline to receive the
building on the ground that the board has
no money to spend. Between the two the
buiidlng. It is declared, is being neglected
and needed repairs are; left undone. Chair
man Black has expressed himself strongly
in the matter, and with the aid of his
colleagues expects to work a reform In
the matter.
When the jury met yesterday a resolu
tion of confidence in Assistant District
Attorney Black was passed by unanimous
vote. This was brought out by a story
printed in a local paper last Saturday,
in which Attorney Black was charged
with having given misleading information
to the jurymen regarding its powers in
probing Into the affairs of the School De
partment. The resolution reads:
The Grand Jury desires to express Its
deep regret at the attack on Assistant Dis
trict Attorney Black in a late newspaper
report of the Grand Jury proceedings. It
disowns any responsibility for the reflec
tion on him, which it considers unjust. It
desires to express its complete confidence In
his Integrity and worth as a public official.

Williams Escapes Punishment.
After coqueting with the ponderous and
unwieldy machinery of the law for more
than two years, Richard S. Willliams,
twice convicted of fraud in the Chinese
bureau, has escaped punishment.
Ills ca3e was called Wednesday morning
in the United States District Court for
its third trial, and Assistant United
States Attorney Banning announced that
Chin Deuck, an indispensable witness for
the Government, had disappeared and
that without his testimony a conviction
would be impossible. Mr. Banning ex
pressed the belief that Deuck had gone
to China, and if that were true it would
be useless to ask for a postponement. He
therefore moved, on the written request
of United States Attorney Foote, that a
nolle prosequi be entered and the prisoner
discharged. â¢
Judge de Haven granted the motion,
and Dick Williams walked out of court
a free man.

Jail.

OFF NIGHT BUT
SPLENDID "AIDA"
PRESENTATION
Two Valuable Stars?
Make Their Debut.
GADSKI AND ANTONIO CEPPI
A LIGHT ATTENDANCE BUT
HEAVY APPLAUSE.
One of the Best All Round Perform
ances of Verdi's Work Ever
Given in San
Francisco.
Ey Ashton Stevens.
For some silly reason or another
every other night seems to be an off
night at the opera. Not as regards the
performance, but the attendance. Two
stars, Gadski and Ceppi, made their
debut last night in "Aida"; each scored
success; the entire production was
sumptuous, delightful; but the immense
crowd that listened to "Boheme" the
night before and to "Faust" at the
opening was only partially present. Ex
cept for the "palco" boxes, which are
all sold for the season, the dress circle
was practically empty, and there was
vacant upholstery to burn downstairs.
The carriage system was in perfect
working order, because there were not
enough carriages to cause half a block
ade. It was, as they call one of these
off occasions in New York, "poor rela
tions' night."
This sort of thing is very discredit
able to the town. What if we do average
up handsomely on the whole season
how are we going to reconcile our long
vaunted love of opera to the fact that we
turn out in thousands only when Melba
is in the cast. Melba is a great artist,
the greatest coloratura singer in the
world to-day, and a surpassing Mimi
and ditto Marguerite. But Melba can
not do it all. There are dozens of
operas whose soprano roles are not
adapted to her voice; and big, noble
operas at that. This notion of bunch
ing the attendance solely on Melba
nights is absurd. Already we have
heard three other prima donnas of the
Ellis company whose work in different
specialties was in each case excellent;
the general company is the same night
in and night out; the New York sym
phony orchestra is the same. This
mode of opera-going is very stupid,
very provincial. It retards civilization.
Those who were in the Grand Opera
House last night witnessed a fine, com
plete performance of a beautiful and
difficult opera. It is doubtful if we
ever have had a better all-round pro
duction of "Aida." In points of or
chestra, ensemble and mise-en-scene
it was in every respect satisfying. And
prodigious personal hits were made by
Gadski and Ceppi.
Even without the knowledge of her
name and reputation you would at once
spot Gadski for a Wagner singer. Her
voice is large, round and sonorous, and
she uses it with the easy Wagnerian
sweep. Her presence Is intense: ges
ture, fitting; and she can hold a pose
with dignity and effect. Except for a
couple of top notes that were slightly
"pinched," her Aida of last night was
tonally perfect. It was splendid in its
dramatic conception, too. rich with pas
sion and poetry and barbaric splendor.
Ceppi divulged a big bouncing tenor of
almost Tamagnoesque dimensions. His
voice has the real robust quality right
up to the toppest notes. It is nothing
like as wonderful as Tamagno's in the
upper register, but taken all together
it is of a more even development, and it
is as clear as a horn, and Ceppi manages
it artfully and without a struggle. He
gave a virile, soldieriy identity to the
part of Radames, and besides singing
the fiercer episodes with copious power
he invested the love scenes with a de
gree of tenderness unusual in a voice
of this character. Gadski and Ceppi
made unmistakably strong impres
sions, receiving, numerous recalls and
many bravos.
De Vries was still under the climate
and Bensuade was the Amonasro. But
for the trusty Bensuade the barytone
end of the season would be in a sorry
plight. He has sung every night so
far and each time with success. His
Amonasro, while a bit feathery, was
still an earnest, consistent character
and another excellent tribute to the
singer's versatility and endurance.
Olitzka had her first big chance of
the engagement as Amneris and made
the most of it, except for the old con
i tralto banality of slurring and "moo
ing" some of the lower passages. Bou
douresque was the entirely ad
mirable High Priest and Steh
man contributed a telling King
of the Egyptians. The pomp
and pageant of the second act were
magnificently managed, and the great
choruses were given with smashing
vigor. The orchestra distinguished it
self as usual under Seppilli, who was
again led blushing to the stage by his
fellow-artists. _
A FEASIBLE HACK SYSTEM.
Police Sergeant Gorman's Scheme Is
Capped With Success.
Sergeant Gorman succeeded last night
in convincing the patrons of the grand
opera that his suggestion for the hand
ling of hacks was the true key to the so
lution of what has proved a "poser" for
the past three nights.
For the first three nights of the grand
opera season the sergeant vainly at
tempted to Inaugurate his system, but
encountered vigorous opposition both
from the hackmen and the public. Wed
nesday night's confusion, however, chiller*
the opposition, ahd yesterday it was
agreed to test Gorman scheme.
Last night the hacks owned by the I*.
S. Carriage Company, Kelly's Carriage
Company and Tompkinson's Hack Com
pany drove in from Third street and
stopped at an awning to the left of the
main entrance. The carriages belonging
to Conlon, Nolan, Mighelson and Harris,
drove down Fourth and stopped at a
point to the right of the main entrance.
Private carriages and hacks of other in
dividuals stopped at the main entrance.
After disposing of their occupants the
private carriages rested on the south side
of Mission street; those of the first-named
companies lined up on Third street and
the others along Mission and Fourth.
After the opera patrons of the first three
stepped to the left and took the hacks
as they came regardless of the ones they
came in. Patrons of the other companies
pursued the same course.
It was an "off night" and an excellent
opportunity was given to test the sys
tem. It proved adequate for the patron
age last night and all parties concerned
are hopeful that it will meet demands for
a large attendance. There were but 100
hacks in use last evening, while on the
preceding night the number approached
500.' , b:â¢ â  â -.: â 
MRS. FOWLER TO GET
HER WIDOW'S THIRD
Legatees of a Dead Capitalist's Estate Are
Willing to Compromise,
Owing to This an Interesting Family Skeleton
Will Not Be Unveiled to the
Public.
The anticipated unveiling of the Fowler
family skeleton will not come to pass.
The grievances of the late D. D. Fowler
that caused him to disinherit his widow,
if any grievance caused this act will re
main the secret of the favored few who
now hold it, for the case will be settled
out of court. A compromise Is under way.
if In fact, it has not already been ef
fected, and by its terms Mrs. Ella Fowler,
decedent's widow, will come into posses
sion of about one-third of the entire
estate now in ligation which is valued
in the neighborhood of $200,000.
The application of D. Farren testa
mentary executor, for special letters of
administration upon Fowler's estate, was
taken up yesterday by Judge Coffey, and,
contrary to expectations, the application
was granted. Mrs. Fowler's attorneys
made no objection. In fact, Attorney
Beatty stated that he was willing that
letters should be granted the executor.
Judge Coffey made the order, fixing^ the
special administrator's bond at $-0000,
and the incident was closed. It was a
quiet culmination to what was thought
would be a bitter contest.
The application of Farren for special
letters of administration considered by
the court yesterday was the second made
by Farren On the 10th inst. he appeared
before Judge Coffey and asked that he be
granted th! Privilege of administering the
estate of the deceased capita Ist. As
wounds upon which he based his appli
cation he deferred to the will of the de
ceased, in which he was named as ex
ecutor' This will was drawn by Fowler,
or rather by others and signed by him,
four days prior to his death, which oc
curred on March 9, and was presented for
Probate the day following the old mans
death It disposed of the estate as fol
lows- To Lizzie Fowler, a daughter
$6000- to another daughter, whose name
was not mentioned, $2500; to two sons F.
5 and D. D. Fowler, $2500 each, and to
Mrs William D. Farren, $2500. The resi
due of the estate was left to decedent s
children, share and share alike. The
testator's widow was cut off without a
The widow prepared to contest the will
on the ground that the document was ex
ecuted under duress and the undue influ
ence of Mr. and Mrs. Farren. It was also
averred that Fowler was insane for some
time prior to his death and consequently
unable to make a legal will; that while
in this condition he was influenced to
make the document, and for these rea
sons the dead man's widow concluded to
bo Into court and battle for a portion of
the wealth which her husband denied her.
Fowler was an old man, having passed
his seventieth year. For some time before
his death he was broken in health and
It is said even unable to transact business
of the most simple character. As an in
stance of this disability it is stated that
'a few' days before dissolution he was
asked to indorse two checks, but the pen
fell from his nerveless fingers.
This fact was also seized upon by Mrs.
Fowler to prove that her husband was in
no condition to make his last testament
at the time he did. According to the story
of several interested parties the marriage
of Mr and Mrs. Fowler took place at San
Rafael about sixteen months ago and the
estate is separate property. It is said
that in the latter part of last year the
wife caused her husband to be arrested
on a charge of insanity and had him
taken before the Commissioners of Lu
nacy, who, upon examination, declared
him' perfectly sane and ordered his imme
diate release. He was then taken by his
friends to St. Luke's Hospital, since which
time he has lived apart from his wife.
â¢ short time before this date Mrs. Fow
ler sued for support, and three days be
fore his death she caused the Sheriff to
forcibly break in the door of the room
where be was living at the house of Mr.
Farren and had him served with the sum
mons and complaint in suit. The family
difficulties appear to have been due to
money matters entirely. !,ââ_..,_,,
With the threat of a contest, however,
peace has. come. After Farren had been
granted letters yesterday Mr. Beatty
stated that the executor and special ad
ministrator had taken the matter of a
compromise in hand and its consumma
tion was only a matter of a short time.
Under the law Mrs. Fowler was entitled
to one-third of the estate, and as she was
not mentioned in the will it is obvious
that she had good ground for contest.
Perhaps those who stood in the position
of proponents of the document saw this
and a peaceful compromise is the fruit or
their foresight and discretion.
HAD HIS FACE SLASHED.
Frank Dennaro âIs Wounded but
Would Not Tell Who
Did It.
Frank Dennaro. an Italian fisherman,
residing at 1314 Stockton street, received
a severe knife wound in the face at Fish
ermen's wharf last nleht during a quar
rel with a man whose identity the Injured
man refuses to reveal.
About 8 o'clock last night Dennaro. ac
companied by two friends, presented him
self at the Harbor Hospital. He waa
bleeding profusely from a knife wound
extending from his left ear across his
nose to the right ear. While the injury
was being dressed by Dr. Deas, Dennaro
was asked as to the manner in which lie
had received the cut and who had in
flicted it. He curtly informed the physi
cian that all he wished was to have his
face sewed up. Dr. Deas replied that it
would be better to give the name of the
assailant in order that he might be appre
hended and punished. Dennaro repeated
that all he desired was to have his in
juries attended to and ho would see that
the guilty party was punished.
From the positive manner in which he
spoke and the decisive manner in which
he predicted that the knife-wielder would
